		<username>patrick powell</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Excellent, but remember it is a film not a documentary</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a non-American, that is as one who isn't necessarily obliged to insist that the United States of America is the best, most upright country since the dawn of time and a gift of God to mankind to boot, I feel I might be allowed to take a rather more dispassionate view of J Edgar Hoover and his life and work. But I am equally not obliged ? nor, in fact, inclined ? to view the US as the Great Satan of Islamist (and, it has to be said, of some liberal American) propaganda.At the end of the day ? and this might well upset those US citizens who are biased to the first view ? the United States is just another country of several hundred which exist on this earth in 2013, although I must admit it is one that, because of its wealth and the size of its economy, plays an important role in the destiny of other modern countries. (And it should also be remembered that the US has several centuries to go as a 'superpower' before it can even be ranked in the same league as the Persian, Roman, Byzantine, Ottoman and British empires. And then some.) This might seem like a pretty obscure way to start a film review, but it is, in fact, relevant.It is relevant because depending upon your conception of the United States ? to put it bluntly, hero or villain ? you might also be inclined to take a quite specific view of Hoover. So, for example, Hoover's ruthless, and quite possibly illegal, hounding of American Communists in the early part of the 1920s when a great many of those on the left hoped to emulate the example of the October revolution and turn the US into a socialist country could well be seen by some as perhaps unsavoury but sadly rather necessary if 'freedom' was to be preserved. Others might take a diametrically opposite view. (I might be best advised here to lay out my own views ? obliquely ? by asking whether Hoover's concern for freedom in the United States was also a concern for preserving the freedom of its black population. I rather think it wasn't.)Then one should also consider the means by which he kept a very tight control on the FBI for nigh on 48 years: reputedly he kept secret files on the many presidents he served (and their wives) and was able to safeguard his position as director of the BOI and, from 1935, the FBI by letting them know that he knew what they (or their wives) had been up to. If true ? and it probably was ? it wasn't really quite the behaviour we would expect of a democrat. So this is the deal: you pays your money and (as we say here in Old Blighty) you makes your choice from the different and widely varying accounts of Hoover, his life and work.That observation brings me to a review of the film proper, and to another observation that a film, however well-made and however well-intentioned, is, at the end of the day, essentially a work of fiction (as of course are plays novels, poems and paintings which like to present themselves as biography). So someone hoping to be given a true account of The Man Hoover, His Work and His Life, could well be sold short.For all we now, it might all be true. And it might all be complete nonsense. Or, taking a more liberal tack, some of it might be true and some of it might be nonsense: the crucial point is that we simply don't know and never will. Oliver Stone's JFK faced and faces the same problem. So who was Hoover? In my view Clint Eastwood does his best to give an balanced account, and that is to his credit.Hoover was undoubtedly a very complex man. Was he gay? Did he (as some claim) like to dress up in women's clothing? Who knows? Were he and Clyde Tolson 'a couple' or not? We don't know. And even if they were, would that have any bearing on his work? Given that he lived in a far more homophobic age, the chances are that if he were gay and wanted to keep it quiet, it might well have done.I repeat: we don't know. So let me now get to the point: is Clint Eastwoods's J Edgar a 'good' film and one worth watching? To that I will give an unequivocal 'yes', and in many ways that is down to the extremely good and entertaining performance of Leonard Dicaprio. I think in man years' time when I have already been pushing for a number of years he will be regarded as one of his generation's best actors (and there's a lot of competition). But he has a good supporting cast.There's one aspect of Eastwood's film I should particularly like to highlight: it is, like all films today, a colour film, yet it is so skilfully filmed and light and shadow are so skilfully manipulated that we might well be watching a black and white film. That aspect of it, and Dicaprio's performance, are typical of high standards throughout, so perhaps special mention should also be made of Tom Stern, Eastwood's cinematographer. I recommend it to everyone interested in J Edgar and well-made films. But please remember that it is, first and foremost, a film, a fictional interpretation of the life of a controversial man.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>How J. Edgar Hoover; the man who turn the small group known as Bureau of Investigation into the FBI, we know of today.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love historical movies, and J. Edgar was just one of those movies that wasn't too boring, nor way far out from the historical truth and facts. It wasn't a documentary, but a form of story-telling based on a historical figure. I enjoy the pace of the story, and like being a giant nerd: love seeing the names and images of historical events and names. Reminds me of Hoffa mixed with Brokeback Mountain. J. Edgar Hoover is dramatized in this movie as we see him as the Appointed Director of the Bureau of Investigation. Screenwriter Dustin Lance Black's narrative moves freely among various stages of J. Edgar Hoover's life and career, framed by scenes in which the aging FBI director dictates his memoirs to an admiring young agent. The movie jumps around through his life from 1919 to 1972. In 1921, he became the Deputy Head of the Bureau of Investigation. He had no wife, no girlfriend, practically no social life, and so, the Attorney General promoted him to Appointed Director in 1924 after Anarchist bombings. This is where J. Edgar (Leonardo DiCaprio) meets his new loyal secretary and first love Helen Gandy (Naomi Watts) and started to keep personal and confidential files on several people in crime or anyone that was after him, he wanted them investigated and a file started on them. Nor does the movie talk about what Hoover did during World War 2, or McCarthy's hearings in the 1950's. Joe McCarthy and Hoover had a very interesting feud that would be great for the film. A lost there. It doesn't even talk about his work against the union strikers, his work against the Mafia, and barely talks about his war on Rev. King and the civil right movement. But the civil rights movement was one elephant that couldn't be sidestepped, even by Clint Eastwood. The movie made J. Edgar think Martin Luther King only as a threat. It didn't explore his racist attitude toward blacks. This final crusade against the public opinion that served him so well finally discredited Hoover and his Bureau of Inquisition. Top it off with his moral blackmailing, while hiding his own inadequacies, and you have a dark portrait of the typical hypocrite-persecutor. Not an unlikeable character indeed. He believed that information was power and used wire-tapping. The organization he created was the introduction of fingerprint files, crime labs and agents. He even invented one of the most vicious, racist, and destructive programs ever to emerge from the U.S. Government: Cointelpro. J. Edgar kept his personal life very private. This movie depicts his speculated personal life as having a relationship with Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer), his FBI Associate Director for nearly 40 years. Whatever went on behind closed doors of their homes will never be known, however in the end, Hoover left his entire estate to Tolson. I think some critics are bashing the film due to its homoerotic. Some critic might view this as homosexual activist's propaganda to promote their perversion or same-sex marriage, but let's remember that J. Edgar was indeed a close-door homosexual. Stop gay bashing. Other supporting cast were great, such as Judi Dench as Anna Hoover, Hoover's overbearing controlling mother. It's shows his fears, a paranoid mother's boy who wants to break out, if it's by creating G-Man comics books and movies, or making this stories more than it seems, desiring a celebrity like status. I love the theme how Edgar couldn't express his love and compensated by channeling his passion into a lust for fame and an obsessive collecting of sexual secrets with which he intimidated prominent political figures. It not a standard biopic but a character study of a paranoid, secretive man. Of course it feels closed in. That's the point! I did have an issue with the old make up for the stars. It really was distracting and I think altered their performance. Tolson, in particular, looks like Boris Karloff's Mummy. Hammer's old man acting is just laughable, but better in his normal age. DiCaprio does a fine job of staying in character including his East Coast accent. Naomi Watts is great, but not greatly noted. While the actors all offered up top notch performances, poor lighting and subpar makeup took away from the aesthetics of the film, while a confusing narrative and humdrum storytelling diminished any impact it might have otherwise had. Perhaps designed to create a visually dark mood to match Hoover's reputation, the scenes are so dark you sometimes can't see what's going on. But that's very far from the main problem. The story, a back 'n' forth between old age and the main events of his life is so choppy that it's often hard to follow. Still, the film inspire you to research more about the man and discover what this man Hoover really did for this country.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Leo Gives A Powerhouse Performance As J.Edgar</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>J. Edgar is a biographical drama that focuses on the career of J. Edgar Hoover,the director of the Federal Bureau of Investigation for nearly 50 years.It stars Leonardo DiCaprio in the title role together with Armie Hammer, Naomi Watts, Josh Lucas, Judi Dench and Ed Westwick in key supporting roles.The film explores the public and private life of one of the most powerful, controversial and enigmatic figures of the 20th century.It was written by Dustin Lance Black and directed by Clint Eastwood.The narrative moves freely from various stages of J. Edgar Hoover's life and career, framed by scenes in which the aging FBI director dictates his memoirs to an admiring young agent. The events that were included were his crusade against supposed Communists; his involvement in the capture and trial of Bruno Richard Hauptmann, who was convicted of kidnapping and murdering Charles Lindbergh's infant son; the creation of the infamous confidential files he kept on his many enemies; his relationship with Clyde Tolson,his lifelong friend, companion, and conscience; and his vendetta against Martin Luther King Jr. in the 1960's. The point of view is not entirely unsympathetic, but while it's clear that Hoover was responsible for several crime-fighting innovations, it's equally apparent that this coarse, insecure, socially inept man remained forever under the sway of his overbearing mother, was only too happy to break the law when it suited him, scolded others with self-righteous vigor, and lied shamelessly about his own heroic exploits. In view of all that, it's easy to understand why Hoover's legacy is controversial, to say the least.Leonardo DiCaprio gives a powerhouse performance as Hoover.No question that he remains one of the most formidable and talented young actors we have around in Hollywood.As for Eastwood's direction, he has long since established himself as a master of his craft, and although the lengthy film has its tedious moments.Also,his direction does not allow the viewer into getting to know the FBI director's psyche due to his cautious portrayal of the man,the lack of energy and excitement in the screenplay and somewhat confusing narrative as it was told in a non- linear fashion.Interest in getting to know the man is essential to fully appreciate it.But nevertheless,it remains to be an admirable and excellent film.Giving it the highest rating is fair enough considering for Leo's portrayal of Hoover alone.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>J. Edgar Hoover. I know enough about American history and American politics to know that the very name was enough to strike both fear and loathing into the hearts of successive American presidents for decades. J. Edgar Hoover. For almost 40 years the director of the FBI, played in this bio-pic by Leonardo DiCaprio.The movie (directed by Clint Eastwood) includes a lot of interesting reflections on Hoover's life and what made him the man he was - a complicated relationship with his mother; a complicated relationship with Clyde Tolson, his assistant director and close friend/lover/whatever; obsessively self-promoting and obsessively jealous of agents who got too much publicity. He's certainly a worthy enough choice for this kind of bio-pic. The movie basically shifts back and forth from the investigation into the kidnapping of the Lindbergh baby in 1932 up to Hoover's declining years in the 1960's and early 70's. There's a great deal of interesting material 9mostly related to the subjects I've already mentioned.)Di Caprio was an interesting choice to play Hoover. To be perfectly honest, I can't say that he really leapt out at me in the role. Armie Hammer was better as Clyde Tolson. The most interesting scenes in the movie are probably those between DiCaprio and Hammer as a glimpse is offered into Hoover's private life (which is still a source of debate.) The movie moved too often and too abruptly between the two time periods, with the result that it didn't seem to flow very naturally to me. Obviously, with almost all of the scenes being set in the two afore-mentioned time periods, there's a lot of material from Hoover's career left out.So, the movie certainly gives one a taste of how complex a character Hoover was, but it doesn't entirely satisfy. (6/10)</fullReview>

		<fullReview>This appears to be another highly accomplished and intelligent work from an "Old Master" that will need a few years to finally gain the recognition it deserves. He runs afoul of "expectations" much as Scorsese did with "The Aviator" (a biographical epic which may be that director's masterpiece...despite it's losing the Best Picture Oscar to Eastwood's "Million Dollar Baby"...how ironic that both that study of H. Hughes and this portrait of J. E. Hoover share not only a leading man but a dazzling sense of period and cinema technique that leaves much of modern film craft in the shade.).One wonders if the critical brickbats and public indifference that contributed to this films lack of recognition were not largely in response to ultra macho "grand old man" Eastwood taking on the subject of Hoover's clandestine and as depicted here, never acted upon homosexuality. Criticism of the film as being confusing and unable to fully chronicle this mysterious man's remarkable life seem like a lazy knee jerk reaction to this director attempting to move far outside his usual range.To me this is one of his most graceful, deft, and rewarding jobs as a director yet...and he helps guide stars Di Caprio and Armie Hammer to their richest and most resonant performances. To label the film as an incomplete documentation of it's subject's existence or endlessly quibble about a few technical imperfections (yes, some of the aging makeup is not 100% perfect seems heartless when the performances coming through the makeup are so brilliant).In summary...if you are looking for gripping drama, some witty high comedy, great acting, and a superstar director proving that he has reserves of sensitivity and dynamism that may well be bottomless you should ignore the quibbles...sit back...and enjoy!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Complicated Man</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a somewhat true story about J. Edgar Hoover, one of the most secretive and complicated men in our history, who was credited with establishing the Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI).The word "somewhat' is important here because we can never really know all the truth about J. Edgar and the swirl of rumors that flourished during his time and even now. However, we are convinced that the most important thing in his life was making sure the United States government was protected from Communist infiltration.The story starts out with J. Edgar retelling his life story to set the (read " his") record straight. I do not recall an autobiography of Hoover that was of any consequence so this was a surprise to me, but no matter - this technique mixed with past and present day events was fine and filled in some blanks that may have been harder to explain otherwise. It is generally agreed that Hoover kept secret files on top government officials and numerous celebrities to be used as leverage when he wanted things his way. His credo was "information is power." After Hoover's death, President Nixon had his offices ransacked looking for those files. The movie makes it clear that J. Edgar was drawn to men instead of women with Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer) as his love interest. There were rumors of Hoover's cross-dressing only after his mother (Judy Dench) had passed on if we are to believe Director Clint Eastwood's interpretation. All was tastefully done and there were no uncomfortable moments in here. What was good? An Oscar should go to DeCaprio's Make-up man. DeCaprio was made to really look like Hoover. Kudos. DiCaprio's speech pattern for J. Edgar was spot on and we know this from newsreels of Hoover back in the day. All the acting performances were excellent. DiCaprio is always deserving of a Best Actor nomination. And, I especially liked Jeffrey Donovan's portrayal of Bobby Kennedy.What was not so good? The film was shot in color, but most of the scenes were in the dark or with low lighting giving the black/white dramatic effect to further promote Hoover's secrecy concepts.What was "somewhat" confusing? There is that word again. I couldn't help wondering if things may have turned out differently for J. Edgar if Helen Gandy (Naomi Watts) didn't rebuff him when he made attempts at romance in that library. Nothing more is learned about her except she became his secretary throughout his life and kept his secrets. J. Edgar leaves his estate to Tolson but, we did see a niece early on and no other family was introduced or noted. We can consider both as somewhat confusing and unexplained loose ends.We may never know the full impact J.Edgar Hoover had on this country, but even with his good and bad traits, the idiosyncrasies, and secrets, his drive to make the United States safe cannot be denied. Simply put, he was a complicated man.Violence: Yes. Sex: No. Nudity: No. Language: One F-bomb uttered by the Richard Nixon character, and not much soft stuff by others.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The story of J Edgar Hoover and what he accomplished is actually very interesting. The faults with this movie are the jumping around in time and the bad makeup for ageing the actors. It would have been so much better if the story had simply been chronological.If the 2 main faults had been fixed it might have been one of the better movies of the year. 		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Pacing and Makeup are the flaws of J. Edgar</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Do I kill everything that I love? J. Edgar is Clint Eastwood's latest biopic that tells the story of Edgar Hoover's life and how he created the FBI during a span of over fifty years. Hoover was a very controversial character, some people hated him and others loved him, but Eastwood doesn't take any sides; he simply allows us to judge his work for ourselves showing us the good and the bad. Hoover didn't have an idealistic life either; Eastwood also focuses on his personal life and exposes some of his secrets. The film was written by Dustin Lance Black, who also wrote the Oscar nominated biopic: Milk, but unlike in that movie, this time the story suffers from a very slow pace. The weakest thing about J. Edgar is truly its pacing. The subject matter is just so wide that the movie fails to focus on just one particular detail and tries to mash everything together with a series of numerous flashbacks. There were some very interesting moments during the story, but they were just too far spread out and most of the film was really a bore to sit through. I wish the film would have focused more on just one or two aspects instead of trying to include such a broad range. By trying to show everything that went on in Edgar's life, we truly end up not knowing much. This movie suffers from the typical case of trying to say so much that you end up not saying anything at all. The film should of focused on a much shorter time period (let's say the Lindbergh case for example which was probably the most interesting part of the film) instead of covering all the specific details. The highlights of this film however are the performance from Leonardo DiCaprio as Hoover, and the art direction which is just beautiful (like in every Eastwood film). As for the story, the movie begins with an older version of J. Edgar Hoover (Leonardo DiCaprio) narrating his memories to one of the agents and telling him to write his memoirs. It was during the communist hunt that J. Edgar found the opportunity to tighten his grip in the law enforcement agency and make important arrests after several communist revolutions began taking place across several major cities in the US. Hoover was completely dedicated to his job and slowly began building what today we know as the FBI. He had a very awkward social behavior, and very few friends. They called him Speedy for the way he talked, and he was very close to his mother (Judi Dench), to whom he looked up too all the time for advice. He trusted very few people, one of them was his personal secretary Helen Gandy (Naomi Watts) and the other his right hand man and personal assistant, Agent Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer), with whom he had a secret love affair despite the fact that he tried to deny it and hide it. After the witch hunt was over with, Hoover's attention shifted to arresting important gangsters and mafia groups, and in doing so he slowly began to build the Bureau. He had secret personal files on every United States president he served under, whether it was Roosevelt or Kennedy. The true highlight of the film and the turning point for the Agency's autonomy came with the Lindbergh case which received a lot of media attention after a child was kidnapped from his own home. Hoover used this case to build prestige for the Bureau and create a fingerprint database of every citizen in order to solve crimes easier. He built the Federal Bureau of Investigation and this is how he did it.One of the most distracting things about J. Edgar is the makeup. Some of the actors really looked bad when they had to play older versions of themselves (Armie Hammer above all). They should've stuck with hiring older actors to play those roles because the makeup really didn't work. It was awful. Leonardo DiCaprio does save this movie however, from being bad thanks to a great performance. He deserved an Oscar nomination, but I guess the film just wasn't good enough. Armie Hammer also was great in his supporting role, but his makeup when playing an older version of himself distracted us from his true performance. The rest of the supporting cast just doesn't have enough time to shine on screen, with the exception of Judi Dench who is as brilliant as usual. Naomi Watts did have plenty of screen time, but she really didn't have a memorable performance. I'd have to say that J. Edgar is one of Eastwood's weakest films, but I am still a huge fan of his work and wish him the best in his upcoming projects. The subject matter in this film is interesting, but the pacing is just poorly done. I recommend it only for DiCaprio or Eastwood fans although I wouldn't be watching this film a second time.http://estebueno10.blogspot.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Origins of Scientific Detection</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Certain figures are larger than life. J Edgar Hoover ranks highest among them in a career that spanned 55 years from 1917 to his death in 1972 at age 77. Leonardo DiCaprio gives the performance of a lifetime as the bull dog who forged a sleepy federal agency into the premier law enforcement agency mastering the art of scientific detection. Only the great actor Jimmy Cagney who often played G-Men could have done better.The story is told as a retrospective account Hoover toward the end of his life dictated to a newly minted agent. The story begins in the Palmer raids following the bomb radicals had planted at the home of Attorney General Mitchell Palmer (Geoff Pierson). "One more turn of the rocking chair and the AG would have been splat," Hoover recalls. Though G-Men would later assume an air of professionalism and scientific dissection of crime, Hoover arms the G-Men with hand guns and does allow the rough stuff on the radicals. The radicals Hoover complains enjoy too much rapport with the public. "What's important," Hoover tells the G-Men, "at this time is to re-clarify the difference between hero and villain."An agent who expresses the reservation that "The crimes we are investigating aren't crimes, they are ideas," is summarily dismissed even though he is a friend of Hoover.No real Americans were damaged by The Palmer raids. Hoover found an ally at DOL (Immigrations) to ensure that many culprits were sent back where they belonged.Neither the Palmer raids of the 1910s nor the excesses of the Roaring Twenties cemented the FBI's position as the nation's premier law enforcement agency. It was the identification of the Lindurgh kidnapper which made the Bureau. And this was no simple task accomplished despite a botched investigation by Jersey State Police and interference from the national hero Charles Lindburgh. In the process Hoover invented scientific detection even if he had to close DOJ's smoking lounge to do so.Dr Albert Osborne: Is that all Mr. Hoover? I have a 2:30 class to teach. J. Edgar Hoover: No, you don't. Consider your pay doubled; you now work for your country. Congratulations, Dr. Osborne.Ever brilliant and always effective in self-promotion, Hoover despite claims to the contrary secretly funded favorable Jimmy Cagney movies about the Feds, giving rise to the Great Myth that Washington can solve all local problems. But he kept his eye on politics or more particularly politicians, to ensure he maintained his fiefdom. By the time of Nixon, Nixon did not even ask to see the confidential files.The film is long and some say it drags. However I might have liked to see Clint Eastwood's spin on how Hoover ended the open mob warfare of the 1920s. More likely than a few apprehensions of some notorious bad guys, the FBI cut a deal with the Mafia to put a lid on the gun battles, kidnappings and bank robberies prevalent throughout the 1920s. Hoover publicly denied that the Mob existed.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Another Eastwood Gem!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If you are expecting ridicule, vilification, or even justification, this would be the wrong movie to watch on J. Edgar Hoover.Eastwood delivers a dark brooding, and rather objective look at one of the most controversial and polarizing figures in America. While the story itself may not be overly compelling, it does offer explanations behind some of the motivations that made up the man, John Edgar Hoover. The cinematography was a crucial element in creating and maintaining throughout a sense of secrecy, brooding, alienation, as well as distance. The chronology is disparate and effective: at times we see Hoover dictating his memoirs to an array of journalists and then we are taken back to the time in question, all the while framed in this overcast mood that fits the film and its subject matter perfectly. As a matter of fact, the manner in which this was filmed constitutes, to me, the biggest indication of Eastwood's opinion one way or the other - Hoover was a dark man with some even darker secrets.J.Edgar's homosexuality as well as his proclivity for cross-dressing are only suggested and are handled with the utmost sensitivity. There is one scene which strongly implies his helplessness with his desires to cross-dress that was simply quite touching. And the relationship between Hoover and Tolson was beautifully written. No, I did not come away feeling empathy/sympathy for Hoover at all; neither were any of my perceptions surrounding him challenged in the least. I did leave the film however with surely a more heightened sense of his humanity.This could NOT have been accomplished however, were it not for the positively riveting performance by Di Caprio. This guy can just ACT, okay? The other supporting players were equally excellent, most notably, Judi Dench who played his overbearing mother, Naomi Watts as his faithful secretary, Helen Gandy, and Armie Hammer, as Hoover's lifelong companion, Clyde Tolson. Some of the movie's most compelling scenes involved these three and all three gave powerhouse supporting performances to match Di Caprio.I highly recommend as yet another excellent outing from a very good director. 		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>The Story Just Isn't Complete</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As others have mentioned, "J. Edgar" is more of a character study than a straightforward biography or documentary, and there are times when "soap opera" might be a more appropriate term. The general picture it paints is one of an ultra-neurotic buffoon, albeit one with a few admirable qualities. Although Hoover may have been a hard guy to completely like or admire, it just seems the creators had some issues with their subject that show through a bit too obviously. Also, the way the film reverberates between public (Hoover's formation of the FBI, Communist subversives, kidnapping of the Lindbergh's baby, etc) and private life (his close relationship with his overbearing mother, awkwardness with most other women,workaholism,cross-dressing and apparent latent homosexuality, etc) gives it a patchy feeling. Granted, coverage of both areas is necessary, and they are not easy ones for a director to synthesize. Nevertheless, some bad choices were made here regarding what to include and what not to. There is nothing, for example, on the prosecution of the Rosenbergs or the FBI's role during the Vietnam War years. I, for one, would have liked to see more on such areas and less on Hoover's family life (or lack thereof) and his relationship with his right-hand man, Clyde Tolson.Despite the long time-span it covers, "J. Edgar" is smooth enough to follow, and Hoover's conflicting qualities--e.g., insistence on professional conduct and limitations of the Bureau's jurisdiction, yet, defiance of his own standards by becoming a sort of Rona Barrett of Washington--are often quite intriguing. Still, a linear biography told from a more distant and objective third person perspective might have worked better as the structure of this film.Most of the acting is tepid, but Judi Dench and Naomi Watts both give admirable performances as the two important women in Hoover's life (even though the make-up is terrible).</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>It's fine but it could be better, almost epic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Grandiosity of production scale, character and intention doesn't always means grandiosity in quality. If this fails a little flat it's because it tries too hard to reduce in a very limited time to paint a fair portrait of one of the most distinguished and controversial figures of America's history. The partnership between Eastwood, DiCaprio and Dustin Lance Black, Oscar winning script for "Milk" didn't live up for much expectation; and if the film works it's because of the work of all the other actors and some of the technicians involved. "J. Edgar" chronicles the life's trajectory of FBI's powerful head J. Edgar Hoover (Leonardo DiCaprio) from his younger years as agent until old age, period in which he was still the big boss of the bureau, up until his death. This movie's huge ambition to stand above another films made on Hoover is to focus on the highly debated yet still slightly proved, Hoover's closeted homosexuality, and having as longtime partner his number 2 in the office, the loyal Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer). Naomi Watts and Judi Dench play the important women in Edgar's life, his personal secretary and his devoted mother, respectively. What's the biggest achievement of "J. Edgar", the movie? To be an brief summary on Hoover's life, to explore , even if superficially, the bureau's creation and the way investigations and cases were conducted. In this little aspect I think it succeed better than "The Good Shepherd" which was about the CIA's creation. At least it was less confusing but not a better film than De Niro's ambitious project."J. Edgar" could team all the involved together; Clint, Leo, Dustin and the others...but the effect would be felt graciously if made as an 6 hour HBO miniseries or similar. And maybe with another director in charge of the project. Mr. Eastwood's choices in conducting this are somewhat wrong, without energy or vigor, an slow pace that often distracts its viewers followed by the ugly dark cinematography he started to use since "Changeling", that doesn't cause any sensation and doesn't help the movie.I don't know how much is fact and how much is theory in here but all I know is that Mr. Black's script hide of what was supposed to be most open and interesting part of the film. Hoover/Tolson relationship isn't explored enough, it's just as closeted as they were in real life. There's pain, denial, several inner conflicts specially when it comes to Edgar demonstrate his emotions to Clyde, it's all well played. But it should have more. Yet, only that kind of approach is inexcusable for a film whose purpose is to dwell deep in aspects of the analyzed life. "What determines a man's legacy is often what isn't seen" says the tag line; movie should show everything that we didn't seen about him in real life. One strange kissing scene during a conflict, or Hoover's detailed attention on what clothes his agents are wearing, and the couple's talks showing some affection between each other isn't enough to characterize an fair and accurate portrayal of the public figure who always knew how to keep his secrets. "J. Edgar" is too secretive with the things it shouldn't been.Does DiCaprio makes a good J. Edgar? Yes, kind of. Far from being my favorite (a tie between Pat Hingle ("Citizen Cohn") and Bob Hoskins ("Nixon"); this comparison is terrible since they're playing in supporting roles) but he displays plenty of qualities and defects. He probably played in the way biographies and relatives described him: well mannered, with a certain shyness but with very authority, demanding, hiding himself from people all around not knowing how to act sometimes, a little pitiful, different from the other presentations of Hoover in films elsewhere. Is it Leo's best performance? No, I'm afraid. It's a good one but not his best. Most of the time I couldn't disconnect from his Howard Hughes role in "The Aviator" (truly his best, in my opinion), all the time he was playing the bossy, fast talking character, giving orders to his staff. It's the kind of mannerisms and twitches he doesn't know how to lose or change. Therefore, he's limited in his range. At least, for this project.However, the huge surprise of "J. Edgar" was Armie Hammer's performance in all the periods portrayed. His Tolson is dedicated, very compassionate and tender, incredibly loyal to his boss/partner and he's a moving character. Not to mention, the old age makeup effects worked better on him than with DiCaprio, such effect was laughable on Leo, not so well made. I argued a lot about its problems but I can't trash it and say it's a bad film. I enjoyed a lot, just felt disappointed with many of its construction. It's a decent and noticeable biography with very informative facts, historical events and a right way of not solving the enigmatic puzzle Mr. Hoover was. I was quite surprised when this wasn't Oscar nominated but after watching I understood why it wasn't. It was good but not all that much to enter in the race. Not that it matters but it helps in getting more audience. And this could make very good use of it. 7/10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>John Edgar Hoover founded the Federal Bureau of Invesgiation and led it in megalomaniac style until his death. This account of his career and speculation of his private life is a dark movie on so many levels. It deals with serious criminality ranging from mobsters to kidnappers to spies; it portrays a FBI that tramples over human rights and itself spies on citizens; it is a portrait of a man who effectively blackmailed presidents but hid his own sexual tendencies; and the whole thing is shot in bleached-out colours and endless shadows.It is an ambitious project for producer and director Clint Eastwood, who has tackled the bio-pic before with "Bird", and writer Dustin Lance Black, who scripted the bio-pic "Milk", since they cover the huge period from the Palmer Raids of 1919 to Hoover's death in 1972 in a series of flash backs involving multiple characters which makes it hard work for the viewer. Of course, it is an even tougher enterprise for Leonardo DiCaprio who has to portray the eponymous and complex FBI chief as he ages half a century. DiCaprio is a fine actor who has come along way since "What's Eating Gilbert Grape?" in 1993 and he too has done the bio-pic thing before ("THe Aviator") and is quite well deserved by his prosthetics (which one cannot say for Armie Hammer as Hoover's No 2 at the Bureau Clyde Tolson).So this is a fascinating film but it lacks a narrative thrust and real drama or excitement.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Robert J. Maxwell (rmax304823@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>History Conjectured.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Initially, it seemed like a TV movie, except for two recognizable performers, Leonardo DiCaprio as J. Edgar Hoover, President-for-Life of the Federal Bureau of Investigation, and Naomi Watts as his loyal executive secretary. DiCaprio sounded like an actor trying to impersonate someone else, Naomi Watts is no longer a teen ager, and the photography was exceptionally dark. Then, without knowing it had been directed by Clint Eastwood, I was gradually drawn into the story, conjectural though much of it must be.Except for his meticulously choreographed public appearances -- and the appearance of appearances -- Hoover was a kind of "gated personality" in the way that some wealthy communities are "gated", surrounded by fences and armed guards to protect whatever it is that's inside. In this way he was a Rorschach ink blot. You can project whatever you like into his private life because hardly anybody knows about it. His personal files were destroyed and unlike many public figures he left no memoir behind.So, in "The Private Files of J. Edgar Hoover (1977)," Broderick Crawford could play him as a good-natured and efficient protector of democracy, hectored constantly by the petty Attorney General Robert Kennedy. In "Kennedy" (1983), Vincent Gardenia had him as a slow-moving but irresistible force for morality seeing to it that presidents toed the line.Eastwood and DiCaprio treat him as a bundle of repression, a man who is fighting what he considers his most base impulses, who loves his mother and has a non-sexual affair with his deputy, Clyde Tolson. There is a tender scene after the two have grown old, Tolson has a stroke, and Hoover prepares his breakfast while lecturing him. It's touching, really. Does Hoover dress up in a feather boa? No. At his mother's death, weeping, he pulls on an old dress of hers and drapes her beads around his neck, then tears all this off and collapses, muttering, "Fight it, fight it." I don't admire J. Edgar Hoover the way Eastwood and his writer seem to. I believe Hoover thought he was surrounded by domestic enemies and bureaucratic rivals but Eastwood seems to think that this was a natural state and the driving force behind Hoover's unorthodox methods was patriotism, whereas in retrospect it seems far more personal and more than a little paranoid.The kind of world envisioned by Hoover looks like that described in Thomas Hobbes' "The Leviathan" (1651). People are born into a state of nature, governed by selfishness and greed. This leads naturally to "a war of all against all" in which the life of man is "solitary, poor, nasty, brutish, and short." We avoid this catastrophe by agreeing to subordinate ourselves to a sovereign authority who rules everything. The sovereign authority is the ultimate judge and doesn't share his power. And who is the sovereign authority in Hoover's vision? Who is most moral, efficient, ruthless in rooting out domestic dissidence? Guess.I applaud Eastwood, though. The guy is in his 80s and is still turning out tasteful and balanced movies like this. Aside from the conception itself, the only weakness I could identify on screen was the sentimentalizing of Hoover's death. I have no doubt many of his loyalists wept. But Eastwood lingers over it for too long and the music is formulaic. Orson Welles never treated Charles Foster Kane that way, one of the many things that makes "Citizen Kane" such a superb movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Shunned by Oscar, it's still worthy of a view on a quiet Friday night instead of Eastenders.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>J. Edgar is a film that, after the initial hype, looks very likely to disappear without very many people (in the UK, at least) seeing it. It's unlikely you'll catch it at a multiplex near you, partly because, at the time of writing, my blog isn't live but largely because this time of year there is a glut of films (great, good, hopeful and just bilge the studios are throwing to the world amongst the others because they have no faith in them) for the cinema chains to chose from. We, the viewers, benefit from this because there's a rapid turnover and great choice. The big losers, however, are the filmmakers that rely on word of mouth for the success of their films. The days of a slow-burner hit are long gone.None of that is to say that J. Edgar isn't a good film; it is. It just isn't great and it's pitched against the likes of The Help, The Descendants, The Artist and The Iron Lady. Two of them are better films, Two are not. The difference is that each of them has been embraced by Oscar and the Golden Globes whilst, despite the early promise, J. Edgar has been completely shunned. At this time of year, with this type of film, that can be damning.I'm not going to outline the plot other than to say it skims over the 48 years of Hoover's career leading up to and creating the FBI and looks into his personal life with the few significant people he kept close. I'm sure you know as much as me and, either way, Wikipedia will give you more detail than I have space for.With Clint Eastwood directing and a principal cast of Leonardo DiCaprio, Naomi Watts, The Social Network's Armie Hammer and the always dependable Judi Dench (as Edgar's mother), it was always going to be a pretty good bet that this would be well worth watching. As biopics go, it's not quite up there with Ali but it's a hell of a lot better than The Iron Lady! It's very watchable, it trundles along at a good pace, is educational to a degree and DiCaprio gives a performance that justifies some of the hero-worship he enjoys, but I left thinking What do I write about it? I expect more from Eastwood. He is, after all, the multi Oscar winning director of Million Dollar Baby and Unforgiven. But he fails to match those heights with J. Edgar.The unevenness of the film is illustrated perfectly in the make up of the two principals. DiCaprio's prosthetic is virtually part of his face, it moves and expresses and is at times easy to forget exists and yet Hammer's, presumably by the same make up artist, sits on his face, unmoving and obvious. It's a mystery how the contrasts are possible in the same film. Maybe it was a lack of concentration or care for the job in its entirety and perhaps that's the problem with the whole film.So put it on your Love Film list as a fairly safe bet for a quiet Friday night in but don't have the high expectations of Eastwood's finest. It's way above Space Cowboys but it falls short of the award-laden Mystic River.Another film review from The Squiss. For more reviews from The Squiss subscribe to my blog at www.thesquiss.co.uk</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>This film reveals not only the persona of J. Edgar, but of Clint Eastwood as well.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film reveals not only the persona of J. Edgar, but of Clint Eastwood as well. As in all of his recent films, we are not offered a conclusion. We are instead given a set of facts, circumstances, and theories, and left to decide the matter for ourselves. The film itself is well presented and the cast is nothing short of formidable. Leonardo DiCaprio creates the impression of growth and personality, showing us the changes from Hoover's past and how they are influenced by his mother and his friends, which are few. The allusion of homosexuality is dealt with in a tasteful and sensitive manner, and again allows us to draw our own conclusions concerning his relationship with his assistant director and companion, Clyde Tolson. Armie Hammer plays his role with sensitivity and passion, as does Naomi Watts. As Helen Gandy, her obvious devotion to Hoover is clear. The remaining cast is incidental, although Jeffrey Donovan gives us another glimpse into his abilities with a flawless performance as Robert Kennedy. At the end, I am left with the impression that Hoover's life was sad and personally unfulfilled, but you'll have to see it yourself to draw your own conclusions. Rated R for subject matter and language, this film is collectible for lovers of Eastwood films, and anyone who enjoys a serious biography.</fullReview>

		<date>2012-02-04</date>
		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Clint Eastwood's Answer to "The Aviator"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"J. Edgar" can be seen as Clint Eastwood's answer to Martin Scorsese's "The Aviator", another biopic starring Leonardo DiCaprio. In both films the central character- J. Edgar Hoover here, Howard Hughes in Scorsese's film- is a controversial real-life individual who rose to prominence in the 1920s and 1930s at a comparatively young age. In both cases that individual is portrayed as a morally flawed individual who also had some genuine achievements to his credit.Hoover's flaws are well-known to history; he was quite prepared to use strong-arm methods and to go outside the law in his pursuit of criminals, he was capable of petty feuds and jealousies, even against members of his own organisation, and was reputed to keep personal files which he used to blackmail prominent personalities, and there is plenty of evidence of these matters in the film. According to the film Hoover had no great regard for Senator Joe McCarthy as an individual, describing him as an opportunist rather than a patriot, but he himself had McCarthyite leanings in that he, like the Senator, tended to conflate political dissent with sedition or subversion. An example was Hoover's hatred of Martin Luther King, whom he believed, quite without foundation, to be a communist agent.On the credit side, the film also points out that Hoover was responsible for creating America's first effective nationwide crime-fighting force, that he was an early pioneer of the use of forensic science in solving crime and that in the twenties and thirties the FBI achieved notable successes against communist and anarchist terrorists and against notorious gangsters like John Dillinger and "Pretty Boy" Floyd. The film is told from the perspective of the elderly Hoover in the sixties, recounting his memories to his biographer, although he only gets up to the mid-thirties; the struggles of the FBI against Axis spies in the forties and communist agents in the fifties are rather skipped over. Doubtless Eastwood felt that the film was quite long enough already and that to include any more material would make it unwieldy and repetitive.One controversial subject the film does touch on relates to Hoover's sex life. There has been a long-standing rumour that he was gay and had a sexual relationship with his close friend Clyde Tolson, the FBI's Associate Director. (Richard Nixon, although always respectful in public, referred to him in private as "the old cocks*cker"). The film, however, does not support this allegation; Hoover is shown rejecting a sexual advance from Tolson, whom he regards as a friend but nothing more. There is a suggestion that Hoover may have been a transvestite, but transvestitism is not necessarily linked to homosexuality. Indeed, Hoover is seen making an unsuccessful proposal of marriage to a female FBI employee, Helen Gandy, who, although unwilling to become Mrs Hoover, was to become his trusted confidante.DiCaprio gives another strong performance, giving the impression of a man driven by a powerful will yet also by equally powerful subconscious impulses. Credit must also be given to the make-up team who succeeded in making the 37-year-old DiCaprio look credible as an old man more than twice that age- Hoover lived to the age of 77- although they were perhaps less successful with the 25-year-old Armie Hammer who is similarly required to portray Tolson from youth to old age. The film, however, is not in my view on the same level as "The Aviator", which I regard as one of the cinematic masterpieces of the twenty-first century. It lacks the epic scope and visual power of Scorsese's film, with nothing, for example, comparable to the heart-stopping aeroplane crash scene. Perhaps the difference between the two films arises from the differences between their two central characters.Although Howard Hughes and Hoover had some things in common, in other respects they were very different. In old age Hughes famously became a recluse, but in the earlier part of his life- the part on which Scorsese concentrates- he was a larger-than-life extrovert. Hoover, by contrast, was an introvert and an intensely private person about whose emotional life little is known for sure, although there has been plenty of speculation, often unsupported by hard evidence. Whereas Hughes was a man of many faces- businessman, aviation pioneer, film-maker and the lover of a number of beautiful women, mostly Hollywood actresses- Hoover was essentially a behind-the-scenes bureaucrat, who often envied his subordinates their more exciting lives. (According to the film he tried to raise his own high public profile by falsely claiming to have carried out arrests in person). Little is known about the most potentially fascinating aspect of Hoover's career, the contents of his private files, as these were nearly all destroyed after his death. He was not, perhaps, therefore the most natural subject for a filmed biography. In making "J. Edgar" as interesting as it is, therefore, Eastwood and DiCaprio have achieved a considerable feat. 7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>silentman01</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>An Incomplete Story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It was understandable to be excited about "J.Edgar" when it came out. A film about one of the most intriguing characters in American History and to top that off, it would be directed by Clint Eastwood, written by the Oscar-winning Dustin Lance Black ("Milk") and would star DiCaprio playing the protagonist. What (at least most people) found was a film which was extremely disappointing due to many reasons depending on their own evaluations. What made it disappointing for me was that it seemed to barely scratch the surface into the life of this man and was thus an incomplete look into a man's legacy (however despicable) and his contribution to American History.No one doubts that Hoover was a fascist who manipulated the most powerful people in America, who threatened his power, by using blackmail to solidify his position as the director of the FBI. However, it cannot be disputed that he turned one of the most ineffective government institutions America had and turned it into one of the most powerful and feared police institutions in the whole world. This he did with no previous police experience which makes this accomplishment even more significant. The film, however, used the relationship he had with Clyde Tolson as the central plot-line (probably in an attempt to humanize Hoover) and by doing so over-looked some of the most significant events which must have made this complicated man as powerful as he was.The screenplay was poor and Eastwood's direction was not captivating and the method in which he told the story fell flat. The film was paced extremely poorly and it's editing was choppy and didn't have the flow which it required for a film which continuously relied on flashbacks to tell it's story. The make-up was pretty horrible and made the "aged-looking" younger actors that much more implausible. All in all it was a bad exercise into film-making with almost every aspect of it's production falling flat and making it into just another disappointing film of what was truly a disappointing year for Hollywood.The only salvation, however small, it had was it's acting. Actors are, in most cases, can be as good as the story that is being told around them. Armie Hammer does provide a very good performance as Clyde Tolson and DiCaprio was believable as the character he was portraying (however terrible their make-ups were) and it made the chemistry seem believable. Judi Dench was not bad as Hoover's mother and Naomi Watts was not disappointing, given that she was not given that much screen time. DiCaprio however, I think did the best he could with the material that he was provided with and at least portrayed the character, as Eastwood wanted him to. with as much realism as could be expected. However, when you are telling a story which is as dull as this film is, there is very little you can do to salvage it from the mediocrity it is bound to be associated to.The ability of an actor to make a mediocre film seem better than it actually was happened only once when Al Pacino rescued "Scent of A Woman" with one of the greatest performances in the legend's career. However, even in that case, Pacino played a charismatic figure who used the good material written for him to his highest ability giving one of the most captivating performances of all time. As "J.Edgar" was lacking in all departments in it's production, it would be unlikely that DiCaprio would be able to do the same. To top that off, DiCaprio is still no Al Pacino. A forgettable film and at least in my opinion, is not worth the viewing. It was even more painful for me because it was directed by Eastwood, who to me made the greatest Western Film of all time at a time when the genre was past it's glory days and then followed it up with films which might not have been as memorable but still held a bar pretty high (barring "Hereafter"). If you are an Eastwood fan, stay away from it, it would bring you nothing but disappointment.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Eastwood has changed. And I think it's just great</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is, if not a masterpiece, a highly entertaining biopic from the old master Clint Eastwood. It depicts the life of J. Edgar Hoover, the revolutionary who took the FBI and made it great, and who also introduced the now familiar fingerprint system to identify criminals. Don't expect an action film. I know the trailer was a bit misleading. It made the movie seem a little more exciting than it really is. It's more like a drama. The story is told from J.Edgar's perspective, as he dictates his memoirs for a book at the end of his life. It tells not only of his involvement with the FBI (specifically the Lindbergh case, which was revolutionary because it finally made kidnapping a federal offense), but also exposes his social problems, his struggle with his homosexuality, and his almost romantic friendship with his right hand Clyde Thompson. It depicts him as a man completely dedicated to his work, who has little attention for anything else, including love. It's amazing the way Di Caprio plays all ages of J.Edgar, 20 to 75. There aren't a lot of actors that could play this role. Also amazing is that the film never gets boring (at least, to me: a lot of people here seem to think it does) even though its almost two and a half hours long and almost nothing exciting happens throughout. That just shows how great of a director Eastwood is. The same goes for Gran Torino by the way, which I think is even better than this.Conclusion: if you're a fan of action/horror films, you will probably not like this one. 		<username>Graham Abraham</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Quite a Patriotic Career for The Leader of the Bureau!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Leonardo DiCaprio has delivered a fantastic performance as he portrays the FBI founder and head J. Edgar Hoover, in the movie of the same name "J. Edgar". Obviously. The film has been brilliantly well done by the director Clint Eastwood. Dame Judi Dench was terrific as Hoover's mother Annie, and Naomi Watts was superb as Hoover's secretary Helen Gandy. Also, Armie Hammer was outstanding whilst portraying the assistant Clyde Tolson. Similar to the flashback style of another classic movie Citizen Kane, Hoover recalls his lifelong struggle and endurance to make the government organization FBI (Federal Bureau of Investigation) a successful authority for the safety and justice of the nation. The threatening and critical oppositions include mob leaders and gangsters, child kidnappers, and American leaders like President Richard Nixon, who notoriously disapproved of Hoover's directives.Hoover's political and authoritative struggles were just as large as his personal conflicts. His mother, regarding her son's homosexuality, threatened that she would not accept him as a son unless he became a more straight and stronger man, interested in the romantic charms of women. She mentored and molded him to be a son to be proud of, she even taught him how to dance. After being trained by his mother who later died from an illness, Hoover was still torn between the two different interests towards men and women. As well as trying to charm Helen Gandy, who only wanted to be his secretary than a girlfriend or fiance, Hoover faced the arrival of Clyde Tolson, who was having a job interview to become an assistant. Clyde eventually showed a loving affection and attraction toward Hoover, and the two became partners, literally. Clyde enjoyed having dinner with him at fancy restaurants, however he argued with Hoover about his involvement with women. Years later in the relationship, Clyde also reminded his friend about his public dishonesty. Hoover apparently lied about arresting suspects, instead having his officers do the detaining work. Despite his flaws, Clyde stood up for Hoover and expressed his companionship.Yes, Hoover also has his victorious achievements: the shooting of John Dillinger, the capture of the baby murderer Bruno Hauptmann, and the deportation of dangerous radicals like German agents threatening the government. Overall, this movie is like a landmark. We learn all about the man who started the FBI, which is currently one of the greatest and productive law enforcement organizations in the United States. So for this great American vision, I give DiCaprio and Eastwood's work a 10 star rating. Watching this film, and also touring the FBI Headquarters in Washington, D.C. is a great way to learn about the methods and brave commitments of this historical authority.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>bites off more than it can chew</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The life of a person as famous, complicated and influential as FBI Director J. Edgar Hoover deserves a mini-series treatment rather than standard feature length, especially considering that a sizable chunk of the running time is wasted on a not particularly insightful or dramatically gripping rehash of the infamous Lindbergh kidnapping case that dominated headlines in the early 1930's. Clint Eastwood directs with a careful?perhaps too careful? hand but the script by Dustin Lance Black bites off more than it can chew in 2 hours and 17 minutes, forcing too much information to be spat out as voice-over narration. This un-cinematic approach results in something like an elaborate show-and-tell session or History Channel re-enactment. What's worse, the film is shot in pale blueish tones that tend to drain whatever passion occurs in the sequences that are fully dramatized. Black does manage to tiptoe suggestively and paradoxically around Hoover's sex life. This is a wise move, considering the conflicting claims made by various people who knew him. The point is made that it's quite possible that the only intimacy he ever knew was what he read in stolen correspondence or heard on surveillance tapes that captured the sounds of sexual trysts by the likes of John F. Kennedy and Martin Luther King, Jr. Most of the story is framed as reminiscence by the title character (played by Leonardo DiCaprio) in conversation with young biographers. DiCaprio's old age makeup is too obvious and photographed too clearly for too long (that of his co-star, Armie Hammer, who plays Hoover's companion Clyde Tolson, is even more extreme, making him look more like Freddie Kruger), so that we are constantly reminded that we are watching a young actor trying to play old. Speaking of old, Judi Dench as Hoover's doting mother looks more like his grandmother, and why would an American woman of Swiss ancestry speak with a British accent? Dench is easy to take, however, because of her essential screen charisma. As is usual with biographical films of this type, we are presented with a parade of famous figures, few of whom are portrayed by actors who really resemble them. Jeffrey Donovan and Christopher Shyer (as Robert Kennedy and Richard Nixon, respectively) are exceptions. The miscast actresses playing Ginger Rogers and her politically active mother Lela miss the boat entirely. They don't even slightly resemble each other. Naomi Watts beautifully inhabits her role as Hoover's long- time personal secretary. Eastwood also composed original music and it is unobtrusive, as is his delicate use of period recordings to lend subtle flavor to certain scenes, although it's hard to imagine that an upscale Washington D.C. restaurant would have been playing a phonograph record of Gene Austin singing "My Blue Heaven" while patrons dined.</fullReview>

More gentle lighting could have been more forgiving on this makeup, but unfortunately it was very flushed out and you could see it was fake which completely destroyed the illusion. Once again after seeing The Iron Lady I expected more from this film on this front.J. Edgar was a very disappointing, overlong and joyless ride. There are some poignant moments here, but they are over shadowed by poor scripting, bad costumes and makeup and uneven amateurish lighting. I expected a lot more from a veteran like Eastwood, but for a convincing portrayal of this historic man Leonardo Di Caprio does deliver, even if the rest of the film doesn't.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Largely Speculation</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Perhaps private lives are best left to the speculation of biographers, where a nuanced explanation of the guess work can be presented. I kept wondering what we know for sure, what we are guessing and what pretty much has to be true, but is still a little speculative. Granted, the broad outlines are known. J. Edgar must have been gay and probably was a cross-dresser (the cross-dressing is not shown in the movie). Seems pretty certain that he did some unauthorized spying and used the results to buttress his own personal power. We know that he hated Martin Luther King, Jr., and had his agents spy on the man. But after that, this movie, for me, raised far more questions than it answered and I kept wanting to take a look at the footnotes, which of course are nonexistent. So it was frustrating to watch, although it is motivating me to perhaps get a biography of Hoover and learn a little more about this fascinating person.To me, there is something a little unfair about publicly guessing about the unknown details. The author is putting his own words in another person's mouth. Nobody can know how close to the mark it is. Yes, this is done a fair amount in the film biz and does not run afoul of any laws. There is an understandable motivation in bringing something that the author feels is important to the national popular attention. A documentary would be very unlikely to succeed at this task. Still, however, the author places himself between Scylla and Charybdis, in that the more interesting (inflammatory) he makes the dialogue the more he risks really creating a false picture of his subject. But if he holds back the effort won't have enough popular success to justify the project.So -- sadly -- a movie better left not made. My guess is that the financial backers, if motivated principally by financial consideration, agree with me.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A well crafted biopic, another impeccable work by Clint Eastwood</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When you have to do with Mr. Clint Eastwood you expect much, and you usually get more than you expect. I was not so much fascinated by this story, before watching the movie, but soon got deeply involved in it since from the beginning. What impressed me was the lucid, at times disturbing portrait of a man, devoted only to himself, to a maniacally rigid public image, fighting against any possible enemy of the legality and security of the country. But Clint Eastwood never abandons his human perspective and tries to go beyond the facade, to look for the tormented psyche of an obsessed man. A man who certainly suffered from megalomania, but was also probably the victim of an insane relationship with an oppressive mother, and had to live with a sexual orientation and a whole emotional life he could only repress. The result is a very strong, but also balanced biopic, where every character, scene, detail, every single word seems to be calibrated and carefully represented.The movie appeals then as a thoroughly crafted character study, but proves to be more than this, in its honest portrait of a whole state-system of national defense, that seems to need a public enemy at all costs, and uses all means, even those which it's apparently fighting, in order to maintain its worldwide indisputable supremacy. A theme having accompanied American history for decades and which is never out of fashion. I will never stress enough Di Caprio's persuasive performance, as well as Eastwood's impeccable direction, but let's also note the skill of Judi Dench, who sometimes steals the show. Certainly a long movie, which goes back and forth in time to cover almost 40 years of history, a very demanding effort for a movie and requiring a certain time-run, but never resulting boring for the viewer, on the contrary capable of capturing and never losing the viewer's attention.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Arrested Development</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is recommended.Mr. J. Edgar ( as in Hoover ) was an iconic symbol of corruption, law enforcement, and good ole American values all rolled up in one very larger-than-life package. He was...A mean one, a heel, as cuddly as a cactus, a nasty wasty skunk...Oops, now wait! I am getting a bit confused...that's Mr. Grinch. Let me start again. He was...Daring and resourceful, led the fight for law and order, a greater champion of justice, with his faithful Indian companion Tonto...Whoa! Wrong again. It's easy to confuse the man and the myth and Clint Eastwood's well made but slightly disappointing film, J. Edgar, tries to clear up some of the facts and fallacies that have befell this crusader throughout the decades. He ( Mr. Eastwood, that is ) doesn't always succeed, but he does create a complex man trying to come to terms with his own power and fame and yet unable to accept his own human flaws and foibles.Leonardo DiCaprio portrays Hoover and runs the vast emotional gamut from a young idealistic and ambitious man ready to conquer the world to an aged cynical and lonely tyrant. DiCaprio expertly hones in on his character, that of a hypocritical man whose own life was shrouded in secrets and lies, an unethical schemer who took great pleasure exposing other peoples' secrets and lies.There is solid support by Naomi Watts as his stalwart and loyal secretary, Helen Gandy and Judi Dench as his manipulative and controlling mother. Yet the star-making charismatic turn comes from Armie Hammer as his closer than closest friend, Clyde Tolson. Hammer adds the needed charm and central moral conscience to the film. This talented actor plays Tolson as more than a handsome cipher or Arrow shirt model. He brings serious gravitas to the role and his dramatic outbursts with DiCaprio are the film's highlight. To put it more bluntly, Hammer nails it. ( Oops, another pun! Sorry! )Eastwood's leisurely paced biopic shies away from the more salacious details of Hoover's and Hammer's relationship, only hinting at and gently suggesting a darker side of his sexuality, something the real man would have never done when investigating any subject of interest. The film's remoteness and inability to flesh out his more personal side weakens this arresting ( pardon the pun ) film. The more intriguing aspects of Hoover's private life remain private and strangely off limits in Eastwood's vision. In fact, the film is so episodic in structure that it never builds to any emotional tension. The real connection to the film's characters remains remote due to Eastwood's vision and the film's arbitrary screenplay. The script by Dustin Lance Black instead chooses to focus mainly on just the facts, ma'am ( another pun, I'm afraid ) avoiding all gossip and sly innuendos as it spans the 50 years of Hoover's career and his rise to become the F.B.I.'s most notorious bureau chief. Some scenes become highly melodramatic and could easily verge on camp were it not for the outstanding acting by all involved. From his implementation of such innovative programs as fingerprinting analysis, wiretapping techniques, and collating dossiers on suspects of both criminal and/or political adversaries, to his misdeeds which include blackmail, extortion, and threatening anyone with an opposing viewpoint, including former presidents and senators alike, J. Edgar glosses over Hoover's peccadilloes whenever possible and remains safely in neutral territory, rather than supporting any conjecture on that controversial subject. The film is dishonest in its honesty.Production values are top-notch, with the sole exception being some pasty and unnecessarily artificial make-up during Hoover's and especially Tolson's elder years. Yet other scenes involving the aging make-up and the prosthetics on many of the characters is quite accurate and believable. However, one would think, with make-up being such a pivotal aspect to the film's credibility, that these flaws would have been worked out before the film process began. To say that the make-up is spotty is an understatement. While it's true that Hoover didn't age well, he certainly didn't age this badly as this film resurrection indicates, doing a major injustice to DiCaprio's fascinating portrayal. DiCaprio imbues the depth of his character, showcasing the anger, paranoia, and uncompromising side of this troubled man very well. However, moviegoers can only linger at Hoover's interfering physical appearance in this film, on the poorly executed prosthetics in the later scenes, which upstages this actor's fine nuanced work. Thankfully, DiCaprio's acting choices captivate and he is mesmerizing in the role.If only J. Edgar would have had a more risky and edgy goal in defining this man and his myth, the film could have been all the more interesting. Instead, it feels a bit unsolved and too pragmatic for its own good. The film continues to gingerly sidestep many prominent issues about the man's personal life as opposed to investigating and confronting these details head- on, leaving loopholes and questions still unanswered. J. Edgar opts to follow a more conventional approach, becoming a spiritless facsimile of its own cold case file. GRADE: BNOTE: Visit my movie blog for more reviews: www.dearmoviegoer.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Leo's brilliant performance makes this a must-see</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"J. Edgar" is the story of one of the most powerful men in America, J. Edgar Hoover, brilliantly portrayed by Leonardo diCaprio. Directed by Clint Eastwood, it tells the story of Hoover's rise to power and of his contributions to the world of law enforcement - a fingerprint database, forensics, helping to make kidnapping a federal offense, wiretapping, and the formation of the organization itself. The script by Dustin Black also takes some educated guesses about Hoover's private life, what there was of it - his relationship with Tolson (Armie Hammer), his mother (Judi Dench), and his secretary Helen Gandy (Naomi Watts.The film switches back and forth in time, juxtaposing the young, ambitious J. Edgar with his older, paranoid self who senses, if he hasn't quite come to grips with it yet, that he's losing his power. He is depicted as a man stuck in his Victorian upbringing, with a formal way of speaking (indeed this was true of Hoover) and a set of strong, unwavering values by which to live, some of which were in direct contradiction to who he was. His closet homosexuality is explored -- and if Hoover was gay, and there seems to be every indication that he was -- he probably was too uptight to have ever directly acted on it or acknowledged it; indeed, he seems to have been homophobic. Some reviews indicated that this was subtly presented. I didn't find it subtle in the least. What I did find is that Eastwood tries to present Hoover's life in a balanced way, showing us a complete human being.The film takes us through some of Hoover's big cases, Lindbergh kidnapping being the main one, with references to the capture of John Dillinger by Melvin Purvis which seems to have been a real thorn in Hoover's side. The movie also covers the deportation of anarchist Emma Goldman, Hoover's work against Communism, and his efforts during the gangster wars.The production values are excellent, showing the U.S. in the '30s, the clothing of the era, and the depiction of gangsters in film, with scenes from "Public Enemy." All in all, a very absorbing and ultimately sad story, with some marvelous performances from the chameleon-like Naomi Watts and Judi Dench. Armie Hammer as Tolson suffered from one of the worst makeup jobs in history, worse even than James Dean in GIANT - I could easily believe at the end that he was supposed to be 90. His is a very difficult role, that of a man more open about his sexuality and treading a thin professional line as well.But the star of the film, the person who commands it, is Leonardo diCaprio. Cursed with the same face he had when he was ten, DiCaprio manages to become J. Edgar Hoover, and his character development and aging are spectacular. He gives us a man of deep and mostly unexpressed emotion whose work was his life, but in the end, the victories seem empty.Really great work from the 81-year-old Eastwood.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Presenting us a hero but not defining hero versus villain</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"J. Edgar" is worth the hype, the fuss and the wait. I was particularly intrigued by the prospect that it was directed by the older, masculine Clint Eastwood and written by the younger, out-and-proud Dustin Lance Black. I got the biographical story of the FBI leader and I also got the deeply-touching love story of a closeted gay man. Both stories were woven together seamlessly.We jumped around in time, and also a bit in reality, to not just tell us who he was and what he accomplished, but to show us why he was that man. That's definitely what he wanted, but I'd wager that the opposite is closer to what he got.American kids growing up towards the end of Hoover's life seemed to think that they were to regard him as a bad man. Mostly because he did things that most people believe are wrong. But if those wrong actions come from a pure, child-like need of understanding good and evil and wanting good to be celebrated and evil to be punished, is that really so bad? He deeply detested politicians, anarchists and communists. I think he also believed that they were frequently one in the same. But he formed the FBI to protect America from any and all crimes. He cared about appearance, decorum, and respect. Mustaches are bad, well-cut suits are good, and he hired and fired on both accounts.The intimate details of Hoover's life (which we probably can't know for sure) came to life in the film when Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer) walked into his office and was immediately hired. Presumably because he's handsome and knows how to wear a suit. Tolson was shown to be more in touch with his sexuality. With Tolson by his side, the drama was heightened to show all the warring elements within Hoover ? how he was raised contradicted with who he actually was and what he believed to be right contradicted with what was actually happening.Leonardo DiCaprio was an impeccable choice to play J. Edgar Hoover from a young man to his dying day at age 77. DiCaprio himself is nearing 40 but still looks the same as when he was a 16 year-old homeless kid on "Growing Pains," and now might be his time for the Oscar. The transformation through all of Hoover's weight gain, hair loss, and infallible belief system was perfect. He showed us the man as he should probably be remembered, and almost as he would like to be remembered.</fullReview>
