		<shortDescription>If Only Scorsese Directed this Monster of History</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The life of this notorious public figure that was hated by many is too by-the-book. The biography lacks any raw energy. The soundtrack lacks any real impactful music score. This is something a better director like Martin Scorsese would have addressed. Also lacking any real momentum is the editing. A Scorsese picture will cut to the meat of a scene and focus on the interaction, then cut away, no loitering around after. Dicaprio is certainly capable of acting. Whether he is perfect for this role is hard to speculate. Too much, however, is spent establishing his different ages via old makeup, to older makeup, to ridiculously old makeup. I think his over-attention to that kind of detail overshadowed what should have been better exploration of themes.These themes were able to be slightly touched upon in certain moments of the film, but got lost in the bloated screenplay. When Scorsese attempted a biopic of Howard Hughes, instead of trying to span his entire life, he concentrated on the most incredible period of his heyday. Here, too much time is spent with Hoover in his ridiculously old age period recalling his important moments, not really telling us much about Hoover the man in the process. For such a far reaching individual, the scope of this too long film is way too narrow, focusing only on Hoover and his two immediate assistants. Some nice Washington figures that factor into Hoover's world could have given this film a badly needed breathe of life. Instead, they are kept out of frame. Only Nixon near the very end of the film is allowed to provide some balance to the one-side Hoover show.It is unfair to the important subject matter to treat this monster with little confrontation. There seems to be some buildup to a climax (Kennedy assassination? Martin Luther King? Illegal Wiretapping?) but none is really given much emphasis. The only fireworks left is the not so revelatory relationship he had with his partner and personal assistant. Sure, it would have been a blockbuster scandal back in the day, but now it has no surprise or bombshell impact. Worse, of all the misadventures Hoover engaged in that could have been better explored in the 2+ hour movie, the unexciting rapport he had with his two sidekicks is by far the most boring. OK we get it, his assistant Gandy was a loyal conspirator. After scene after scene of Hoover and his partner Tolson, not much else comes out of it. All that is left to explore that would have remained in Scorsese's version are the scenes with Hoover's mother. Clearly, a great deal about who Hoover was came from her, and at least all parties involved get that right.Eastwood, let's face it, is an old guy. His direction is a little wooden. There is little flair or momentum to his recent films. The lack of a powerful soundtrack absolutely sucks all the emotion out of the film. I am reminded of "The Good Shepherd", where an important story that is integral to our country's history, is treated too benign in fashion, when a real piece of history should have a visceral impact on us. Such moments can be found in this screenplay, but they are buried in a pile of less important dialogue that passes without any comment. Although significant characters in history are involved, they never show up in the film. Scorsese and Decaprio, the duo that brought us the excellent "Aviator" and "The Departed", would have been a perfect match for a truly scandalous biopic. The single fact he could hold his seat of power for 40 years or so should spark controversy alone, but just goes by without really being addressed. The audience loses the chance to feel the impact of that fact with this blah by-the-book storytelling.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Eastwood is No Kubrick</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this film (finally) on Netflix and I found myself glued to the screen for two and a half hours--not entertained, exactly, but fascinated by the way that so many talented people could produce something so "not good." This movie is not really good. It's acceptable, but not special enough to deserve more than a six out of 10, and that's including two stars for the script, which was clever and concise, and that's high praise for a movie script.The problem with the movie wasn't the cast or the script, it was the director. This film needed a firm hand on the wheel but there was no such guidance. It just drifts off message and runs into the rocks. It reminded me of "Hoffa," which also featured a weird biography of a famous (infamous?) character, that was fascinating because it was so weird. "J. Edgar" desperately needed to show us something about, well, J. Edgar. It needed to demonstrate just how he rationalized his crimes and emphasized his heroism. I think the script originally attempted to do that--to show a tortured soul coming to grips with the distance between his reach and his grasp, between his actions and his motives, but the execution is off. We needed to have a pause or two in the action, where "Speedy" Hoover would slow down, or even stop, the camera could give DiCaprio an opportunity to convey the internal hurricane that could result in the man like J.Edgar Hoover. But Clint Eastwood's direction never takes a break and we speed along like a cheap tour bus of Famous Homes of Washington--never pausing to take a breath and never having the chance to imagine what it must have been like to be one of the most famous men on Earth and, also, one of the most secret.This movie was a missed opportunity. Clint Eastwood seems to produce some mighty fine motion pictures when horses, horsepower and shooting and punching are on the menu. When the evil that men do is quietly done by those wearing spiffy suits and ties, he's out of his depth. This film suffers from too many answers and not enough questions. To understand a man like Hoover, a few good questions go much further than all the bogus "answers" in the world. Somebody, at some time, during the filming of this should have made the "too on the nose" gesture and told Clint not to be so literal.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Rather dull, and not really clear as to its purpose</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Once upon a time, when everybody still looked like they had stepped out of a picture by Edward Hopper or Jack Vettriano, there was a rather weird guy who built an empire which he then pursued to the point of obsession, at the expense of all else and in a way that actually did himself harm and hurt many others... Let me guess, a never-really-bettered film made with (and by) Orson Welles 74 years ago? No. OK, then maybe Howard Hughes as portrayed in "The Aviator" by Leonardo DiCaprio? No, but (very) close - J. Edgar Hoover as portrayed in the film of the same name by Leonardo DiCaprio! Supplementary question: how many more times is Leo going to age slightly unconvincingly in a movie? Answer came there none. To be fair, I learned some things about America I did not know from "J. Edgar", most especially in the first few minutes of the film about the period immediately after the First World War. However, for the most part (about 2 hours) we get to know a bit about a rather unpleasant man doing rather unpleasant things in what seems like a rather unpleasant country. This is actually a bit unfair on the FBI, since obviously they must have done plenty of good stuff. (So much in fact that the "X Files" and especially (Olivia Dunham in) "Fringe" make this reviewer personally feel regular regret that he did not sign up for a job at the Bureau years ago!) But here, Hoover tries to protect his position - obviously in the name of America, its Constitution and people - from just about anybody he feels like, and also from politicians out to get him who publicly might seem like heroes, and indeed say much themselves about America, its Constitution and people, but privately are at best flawed and at worst criminal, crooked, merciless or all three. Right and wrong simply melt away in such contexts, and we cease to care. All the more so, when the general atmosphere of uncertainty about America's democratic credentials leave us wondering if the film itself takes a slant we are not aware of! These are near-unsurmountable problems where a film has to make us care about at least something...If I want a history of the FBI, I'll read a book - hopefully one written by a Brit. If I want more about Kennedy or Martin Luther King, well there are films on that (documentaries and otherwise). For all its flaws (and length!) "JFK" is a true work of film art. If I want to know about America in the 30s-60s, I could go with that similarly fine film "The Butler", which takes its stance, knows what it wants to say and sticks with that, in impressive if controversial manner. If I want to see what a bad guy Hoover was, this film won't do it, given that everybody else is bad too, and maybe even America as such (sadly enough, given its superficial reputation). If I want to get to know Hoover as a person, I need less of the history and more of the man - even if they are rather inseparable. If I want to know that - beneath it all - Hoover was a good guy, then this film also won't cut it, despite the odd tender moment. If I want to know that Hoover was a real person, warts and all, this film is not the best vehicle for that either, given that it seems fantastic in places, not least because of that makeup department, in which "warts and all" are what we get, absolutely literally!Sadly or happily, films are not books, and they have to take some kind of line at some point, and not just splurge together past, present and future, private and public life, institution and man, and hope that some kind of sophisticated analytical picture emerges from the chaos. For it does not, and there is just the chaos...</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Pleasant Surprise for an Obscure Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I watched this movie after picking it up on Blu Ray at Big Lots for $3.00 recently. I had never heard of the film, and that made me skeptical about it. But, it was a historical drama with Leonardo DiCaprio and it was directed by Clint Eastwood, and it was only $3.00, so I decided to take the risk. And I'm glad I did!In light of all the above, I went into this movie with low expectations. It's not every day that a DiCaprio film flies in under the radar without making major headlines, nor does an Eastwood-directed film perform poorly at the box office very often. For some reason this film did just that, which is really unfortunate because it's a great movie. Would I call it one of DiCaprio's best? No. And I might say the same for Eastwood, although it is still a very well made film even if it might be second tier to "Unforgiven", "Million Dollar Baby" or "Mystic River".One thing that does make the film a bit odd is that DiCaprio's portrayal of J. Edgar Hoover has him going back and forth between a young Hoover and an old man. From the outset of the movie, you quickly see DiCaprio made up as a man in perhaps his 70's, and I will say that my first take on the first viewing of him like that left me thinking that they could have done a bit of a better job with the makeup. However, as the film went along, either the makeup got better or I got more used to it, and by the end of the film I had a better appreciation for the time that had gone into the transformation.That said, the film itself was quite educational. I'm ashamed to say that I didn't really know very much about J. Edgar Hoover at all before having watched this film. As a Generation X-er, Hoover's story all happened before my time, and my experience of U.S. History is that folks my age got more exposure to the great wars of the 20th Century than we did to 20th Century domestic affairs in school. "J. Edgar" was a great learning tool, even if the film is more of a dramatization than a documentary.That said, I'd give the film a 7/10. It's not the best film you're ever going to see, but it's also a pleasant surprise if you go into it with low expectations.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>In three words; Good ? Bad - Ugly</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>We follow J.Edgar Hoover throughout his life, the story is told via Hoover writing his memoirs with the assistance of various upcoming FBI agents whilst he also carries out his duties as head of the FBI. Eastwood dances around various stages in Hoover's life in perhaps an overly complex way. Clearly the director didn't want to plod through the story in chronological order however; at times I felt that he needed to be more cohesive with the narrative. There were times where he lost the pace of the film.I knew very little about Hoover before watching the film however he clearly led a full and interesting life. What strikes as rather odd from the first scene in why Eastwood would have chosen DiCaprio to play this multifaceted character in the first place? He is not the obvious choice however his acting ability shines through and he is believable even when in full make-up as the older Hoover. Having said this I felt I needed time to get used to his accent and character but as the film settles it is easy to enjoy a very good performance.We have to rely on DiCaprio to hold the film together as he has a poor supporting cast. Armie Hammer plays Hoover's close assistance Clyde Tolson. He also has full make-up in order to age him at different parts of the film however; unlike his co-star he is unable to make a believable transition to an aged man. Hammer's portrayal of old Tolson would not be out of place in a skit on Jackass but here he's a sore thumb.I really liked Hammer in The Social Network as the Winklevi twins however he is clearly out of his depth in this film. Tolson was one of only a few people that Hoover felt comfortable with and could become himself. However, there is reluctance for Hoover to portray his true feelings despite Tolson's drawn out advances and Hoover's own feelings. I feel that Eastwood was as uncomfortable in dealing with Hoover's sexuality as much as he struggled with it during his life.In one scene with Hoover and Tolson the best Eastwood can do to highlight their intensifying homosexual relationship in a conversation about woman's clothing and what I can only describe as a hissy fit! This is condescending to the audience and rather embarrassing. One would expect a better scene from Dustin Lance Black, the writer of Milk.Equally as disappointing was Naomi Watts who plays Hoover's trusted secretary Helen Gandy. She struggles to portray an older Gandy and comes across as constantly being completely fed up however we are meant to believe that she is a loyal servant to Hoover. This does not portray the relationship she had with Hoover very well and ultimately lets the film down.Whilst DiCaprio has to bind scenes together with these poor performances from his colleagues he is saved by Judi Dench who plays Hoover's mother, Anna Marie. Whilst their scenes together are much better than that of Hammer and Watts we still see a struggle in building a believable relationship. Hoover was, for want of a better word, a mummy's boy and Dench does well to show this however the script again lets the scenes down as far too often we hear Hoover saying 'yes Mother' 'no Mother'. It all became a bit Norman Bates to me.Eastwood is very much a hit and miss director for me, at times he is highly overrated not only as a director but as an actor. Fortunately he does not make an appearance in this film. Generally the look of the film works my only criticism is the numerous scenes with Hoover standing on his balcony watching the latest President being inaugurated. Whilst CGI is seldom used in the film, and in these scenes it is minimal, it still does not compliment an otherwise beautifully filmed flick.Eastwood is unable to bring his cast together and perhaps he has relied on DiCaprio too much and he is an exceptional talent but not even he can save the film. There is Oscar talk for the leading man which is fair enough but there will be no awards for Dirty Harry, the remaining cast or writer.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>DiCaprio's Crowning Performance in a Mediocre Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While watching J. Edgar I had nostalgia for the 2008 film by Clint Eastwood, Changeling. Eastwood coincidentally tackles the same decade with that film as he does here with J. Edgar. At times, it felt like sequel to Changeling in a very strange way. Sam subject matter, same cars, same sets, same costumes, Jeffrey Donovan; but, Changeling is a superior film to J. Edgar. Though it features one hell of a leading performance, J. Edgar is a dead movie and one of Eastwood's worst directing efforts.The movie does offer some insight to J. Edgar Hoover's life and celebrates the man who moved our country forward. Things in today's time we forget weren't around 100 years ago. We travel down Hoover's historical life through flashbacks, but this approach doesn't work for the film. It almost feels like "story time"; very convoluted and amateurish. J. Edgar does show some of the brutality of the time, but the film frequently asks us where would we be without the man?The film's undeniable asset is the performance of Leonardo DiCaprio's career as the title character. DiCaprio has always been one of my favorite working actors, sadly after rewatching some of his best work, my opinion of the actor slowly began to dwindle. After my viewing of J. Edgar though, DiCaprio has reaffirmed what I believed to be true. DiCaprio has tackled a lot of yelling scenes in his career; roles that allowed him to go over the top of the overt. But in J. Edgar, he dials it back immensely and it works to his advantage in capturing Hoover's spirit. Setting the voice aside, he really has nothing to really flaunt. It's all created in the scene, within the soul of the man coming out in the actor.DiCaprio's stunning portrayal is what got me through the duration of the picture. He just as much chemistry with Armie Hammer as he did Kate Winslet (times two), Marion Cotillard, and Claire Danes. It's the crowning work of DiCaprio's short career so far, too bad it had to be in a mediocre film. Armie Hammer is really great in his role, too. Namoi Watts is handed a horridly written role, but goes head under water to make her performance the most it could've been. Judi Dench also has a few scenes, but doesn't leave an impression. J. Edgar may have actors giving it all they've got, but it's as flawed of a film as a guilty person on death row. Clint Eastwood usually knows how to command his pictures, but must've been asleep while filming J. Edgar, if he wasn't, he certainly put me to sleep. He drains any life the decent screenplay had to offer. The big thing that distances the audience from J. Edgar is the horrible cinematography. Murky and mud-like, the cinematography makes the film look worse than what it probably is. The film score, which Eastwood perfected in Changeling, is embarrassing and awkwardly placed here.Narrating the movie was a great way to keep the audience aware of the plot, but the way Eastwood does it in a lame way. The script has good notes of our history, laws, rights, and organizations that ran the world at the time. It appropriately shows Hoover's rise to power, change, and innovative ideas about the justice system. The film itself benefits from the occasional style J. Edgar supplies with shootouts, Hoover's handwritten signature, and many genius make-up scenes. In the movie, we see Hoover living momentous occasions such, but after so many pile up you begin to wonder if they wanted to explore Hoover's reactions or if they were just too lazy to try.I didn't know of the possibility that Hoover may gay. There's never a straight answer given to the public (some were even offended when the film was released questioning his possible homosexuality?), but this is one area where Easwood got it right. It gives us a straight answer of yes, he was homosexual. It's more complicated than just gay or straight because he denied those pent up feelings to retain his mother's approval. The scene where the mother and son have that conversation was chilling. I'm glad the bio-pic covered that topic with great detail, showing one of the most valuable men in the history of our country was homosexual. J. Edgar may be a dull film, but sometimes it stretches further than boredom. Rating: 4/10Grade: C</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>It's Interesting but Also Incredibly Boring (Sorry)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Man, I wanted to enjoy this movie so much more than I did but I guess I should've known from the start that it just wouldn't be my sort of flick. I've never been a major Clint Eastwood fan. Sorry, I'm just not. He's too dry. I enjoyed MILLION DOLLAR BABY, but not enough to warrant watching it a second time. Outside of that, he's just never been my style. Add to that the fact that this movie is a biopic on a political figure, which comes with it's own level of expectation of disinterest. But I was really hoping this one would be different because a) I've really come to enjoy Leonardo DiCaprio as an actor and b) the story behind the formation of the FBI and its infamous founder sounds awesome. But alas, it wasn't. Despite everything this movie had going for it, this movie was just sooo booooring. And what's worse than a boring movie? A boring movie with a runtime of nearly two and a half hours. This was a true test of endurance for me to sit through. It's the story of J. Edgar Hoover, the man who resided as the head of the FBI from its formation in 1935 until his death in 1972. It tells of how he formed the foundation we all know today, pioneered new forensics techniques crucial to solving crimes, and collected secrets on the most powerful people in the country. It also touches on the unconfirmed homosexual tension between Hoover and his second-in-command, Clyde Tolson.I hate to discourage anyone from viewing this movie based solely on my negative feelings with it, but I watch all movies (including biopics/historical dramas) with the sole intent of being entertained. Films that are historically accurate are a bonus (and, for the record, I have no idea how accurate this film is to real life). In the case of J. EDGAR, I was bored to tears so many times in this film that I'd lost count. It's a very interesting movie and it would've made for a great subject for a History Channel documentary, but the movie just doesn't cut it for me. Now I may not be an Eastwood fan but I will agree that he is a masterful filmmaker. This film looks amazing. Visually, it's incredibly beautiful. Eastwood (and of course, cinematographer Tom Stern) have an incredible eye for film and every frame is wonderfully shot. There is great production value in this movie, from the sets to the costume design. You'll feel as if you've stepped back in time to mid-20th century America, but the story (encompassing a span of nearly 50 years) is so drawn out that I couldn't help but lose interest. I do like that the Lindbergh kidnapping was integrated into the film as the key case that brought attention to Hoover and the effectiveness of his FBI. It's a case I'm not all too familiar with, but know enough of it that engaged my interest in the story from that point.And the performances in the film are not to be doubted. Like I said, I've come to respect DiCaprio as an excellent actor and he lives up to it here. To be honest, I had a hard time buying him in the old age makeup for Hoover's older years. He looked older, but still sounded like a young DiCaprio so I wasn't buying into it. The same for Armie Hammer as Clyde Tolson. The old age makeup in this movie just didn't work for me. It looked as if they were wearing rubber masks, and their voices sounded no different so it never clicked with me. I was able to get over that fact about 2/3 of the way through the movie. As for the performances, they really were great. DiCaprio should at lease be nominated for some awards and Hammer might deserve a couple nods himself. I almost didn't recognize Naomi Watts as Helen Gandy, Hoover's secretary and confidant. And of course there's Dame Judi Dench adding her strength as Hoover's overbearing mother, which brings us to another aspect to the film I wasn't sure they'd touch on: the one bit of information I retained about Hoover from a high school research project I did on the man was that he was a rumored cross-dresser. The film does touch on his possible unspoken relationship with Tolson and there is a brief inclusion of Hoover's cross-dressing, but it doesn't steal the spotlight. The focus remains on Hoover/Tolson and the birth of the FBI, but it's only barely interesting enough for me to recommend. Watch the film, but expect the need for breaks.</fullReview>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Brokeback Mountain 2: G-Men...where's the rest of the story?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think the thing that disappointed me the most about J Edgar is that it isn't a historical epic. This is NOT about Hoover and his life, or at least it doesn't focus on that as a main topic. The casting for the film is strange at best, and the story is told as almost a fairy tale, taking well documented rumours and putting them all together to tell the story. It also takes historical liberties by using factual historical moments and skewing them to fit this particular telling of Hoover. An example which would be the Lindbergh baby kidnapping which this movie has Hoover himself arresting the man who would eventually be killed for the crime, something that never happened. In fact I think if you asked Hoover himself he would probably not even put the Lindbergh kidnapping in his top ten best cases but they use it as a pinnacle in this film. I'm not trying to be nit-picky because a film like this is indeed fiction so I can handle it not being completely accurate but it takes such liberties unapologetically and never even tries to nail down any truly factual moments in Hoover's life.Perhaps one of the biggest stars on the planet, Leonardo DiCaprio really gives a powerhouse performance in this film. Is he playing Hoover? No not really. He's playing someone that vaguely resembles Hoover in personality but not even remotely close in looks. If he had been playing some random fictional character his performance would have been THEE performance of the year. He is excellent. Almost the same could be said for Armie Hammer who gives a solid performance as Clyde Tolson who is reduced to nothing more than Hoover's lover in this movie. We don't see anything of what he did for the FBI, or his dedication to his job parallelling that of Hoover's. He is literally reduced to being Hoover's lover in this movie and nothing more. Naomi Watts plays Hoover's most trusted secretary for his entire career, Helen Gandy. If you blink you'll miss her in a handful of scenes but the ironic thing is...this was Helen Gandy. She was always there, always by his side, always doing everything, but always in the background so in that aspect they nailed the character. The supporting cast are all good in their respective roles but ultimately are just background for DiCaprio and Hammer's love affair. I do have to mention the casting for the character of Robert Kennedy and Richard Nixon. Nothing against these two men that portrayed them but neither one were good casting for the roles.Anyone that knows me or follows my reviews know that I idolize Clint Eastwood. He is one of my favourite people on the face of the earth. I'm just not sure where he is going with this film. You watch the special features and he says that Hoover's personal relationships are for us to decide what we believe but this film leaves nothing to the imagination. It is plain and clear that Tolson and Hoover were lifetime partners and lovers. If this film had been presented as exactly that...a romance...a fictional 'what if' about Hoover and Tolson...even if they had called it "Hoover and Tolson" then I could review it as a romantic historical epic but it is presented like the life and times of Hoover and it is not. Yes you get breezed through his career and it makes a nutshell of every political relationship he had and it just barely touches on the brilliance of how Hoover stayed vital in American History for decades but then the main story is the romance. I understand now why critics chastised Eastwood for the film because you aren't expecting every other scene to be about his sexuality. That was one tiny tiny part of this man's existence. Now to pay homage to the good in the film. The special effects, make up, and cinematography in the movie is breathtaking. The way they aged DiCaprio, Hammer, and Watts is absolutely brilliant. I've never seen it done so well and so flawlessly. The way that the world comes to life and everything is shot is flawless. I wonder if Eastwood even read the script or looked at the content of the story or if he just lined up shots and worked on the outer workings of the film.So in short...some powerful performances, brilliant make-up and wardrobe and good cinematography but historical accuracy is completely skewed, very little fact based reality, and a love story between two men. Much disappointment for me in what could have been a historical epic. 6/10</fullReview>

		<username>tomgillespie2002</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Fails to explore the ambiguities and psychological complexities of the man</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The first half of the 20th century was an incredibly corrupt and conspiratorial period, particularly in American politics. A figure within this rich history was John Edgar Hoover, founder and head of the FBI from it's inception in 1935. He did much for the modernisation of police procedure, but he was also a very contentious character. He kept secret files on public figures, using them to blackmail. This also kept each new president in check, creating a position in the country who was above the law, unaccountable, and practically untouchable. His FBI was corrupt, and Hoover himself publicly stated that organised crime did not exist on the level that was claimed about The Mafia. It's interesting that it was not until Hoover's death in 1972, that the US supreme court et al began investigating and eventually prosecuting organised gangs.Obviously this first paragraph is unable to summarise the full complexity of a man, - speculated as being homosexual (something that has never been proved) - I was expecting good things for a project directed by Clint Eastwood (who has been active over the last decade or so, producing some incredibly accomplished films), and starring Leonardo DiCaprio (who has himself been involved in great films, and performing spectacularly - despite still having the "heart-throb" label attached to him due to one film, Titanic (1997). J. Edgar's main focus is not necessarily the full complexities of the real man. It's attention is focused upon the man as a public figure, a character that he plays himself in life. The image is important to him; he is shown as being a great PR man. In the early stages of the FBI, he influenced the content of radio shows, comic books and movies (such as 'G' Men (1935)) that were depicting the courageous activities of the bureau. Hoover's public image is portrayed as being practically identical to his private persona, keeping only three people close to him: His mother Anna Marie (Judi Dench), his secretary (Naomi Watts), and Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer), his close associate at the FBI. Tolson and Hoover were close their entire careers, leading to the speculation that they were lovers - something that seems plausible on the evidence, but has no concrete conclusion. Therefore, the film only really briefly suggest this, leaving the viewer to ponder personal conclusions.Whilst the screenplay (written by Dustin Lance Black, who had previously won an Oscar for the fantastic Milk (2008)) fails to really explore the ambiguities, or even much of the psychological complexities of Hoover, the production is exceptional. The film's span brings it through the 1920's into the 1970's and costume, sets and cinematography is dazzlingly beautiful, and exacting of their periods. DiCaprio's performance is good, but the screen is quite often filled with prosthetically 'enhanced' pensioner's, which at times actually aggravated me. This is not to say that the make-up is bad, it is very good. This could have been less distracting had there only been one character, however, with three or more, is too much. With some great talent attached to this project, I would have expected a more interesting film. As it is, it's not the worst biopic that I have ever seen. However, for my money, if you want a film about this interesting historical figure, I would advise you watch Larry Cohen's excellent low budget The Private Files of J. Edgar Hoover (1977).www.the-wrath-of-blog.blogspot.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>saschakrieger</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Hero, monster, human</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Making a film about the life of legendary, and to many, notorious, FBI director J. Edgar Hoover, in his time one of the most powerful people in the United States, is not easy. Too divided are opinions about the man, too high are the emotions running still. Trying to portrait of Hoover is an attempt at which one can only lose. Clint Eastwood has tried and he has at least not failed entirely. As flawed as the film is, it at least presents a sketch, if not a full portrait, which offers food for thought and discussion.The fanatic control freak driven by equal fear and hatred of everything he considered extremist is here reduced to an insecure, complex-ridden, Oedipus-like figure trying to compensate for his own self-disgust. It is a view that mostly reduces Hoover's motivation to the private, his low self-esteem, the adoration and fear of the dominating mother (memorable: Dame Judy Dench), his problematic sexuality. This Hoover is less a political fanatic than a man seeking attention, ultimately seeking love, yet always half believing he does not deserve it. For the right he might be too unheroic, for the left too human. This Hoover, created by Eastwood and brought to life by an impressive Leonardo DiCaprio may be sitting between all chairs, but there is some plausibility to him.At the heart of the film are his two major relationships: with his mother and with his friend, assistant and love of his life Clyde (Armie Hammer). Hoover's rumored homosexuality is hinted at but not finally resolved. they clearly are in love but in what way remains open. DiCaprio masterfully portraits the uncertainty, the paranoia, the aggressive dominance and, yes, even the vulnerability. As flawed as he was, as dangerous as he may have been - and the film does a fine job of allowing glimpses into his dubious methods, especially towards Democratic presidents such as Roosevelt and Kennedy, he was a human being who knew pain as much as he knew love.The film's problems lie elsewhere: Eastwood often excels at giving his films a very distinct atmosphere which allows the viewer to experience how the times in which the film takes place may have felt like. He fails to achieve this here. This is as best costume drama, the paranoid atmosphere of the 1920s and later the Cold War or the gangster folklore of the 1930s are talked about but not recreated. The film also fails to have much of a development. Early on, the themes are set, most of what comes later is repetition. Hoover is not allowed to grow and so the film with its different chronology - it tells Hoover's story until the 30s and then picks up in his later years, the earlier parts subject to Hoover's telling them to prospective biographers - let it unravel into the episodic, the fragmentary.J. Edgar is too long, especially the last 30 minutes are increasingly painfully repetitive. Only at the very end when some of what we have seen before is revealed to be Hoover's lies, is there a hint at what this film could also have been: an intelligent study of appearance and reality, about a man writing and re-writing his own life. As it stands, it is a convincing sketch of Hoover but little more.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>J. Edgar - and Norman Bates</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is very much in the style of Eastwood's 2008 CHANGELING but not quite as enthralling. As in Meryl Streep's recent take on Mrs Thatcher, Leonardo DiCaprio spends a lot of his screen time playing seriously old (amazing make-up!). The flashbacks to his life and achievements are given more depth than Margaret's but here too the emphasis seems a bit skewed. There's a lot about the Lindbergh baby kidnap case (for which Hoover falsely claimed more credit than he was due) and plenty about his relentless pursuit of US Communists, but not enough about his fractious relationship with the Kennedys (and other administrations) - just a brief scene of him listening to a JFK bedroom tape on the day of the president's trip to Dallas.Edgar's momma's-boy closeness to his clingy old ma (Judi Dench) is sensitively brought out, although the context of the single "little black dress" moment (which I was impatient to see!) tends to evoke the spirit of Norman Bates. Hoover's relationship with his FBI personal assistant Clyde Tolson is covered in depth but a bit coyly. There are some cosy domestic scenes (they lived in separate homes) and a bit of discreet hand-holding, but we seem to be invited to infer that the 'affair' was platonic (although Richard Nixon is allowed to suggest otherwise in a particularly fruity moment). There's a pleasingly cringe-making scene when a starlet asks Edgar for a dance and a nice edgy row with Clyde when Edgar lets slip that he's been dating Dorothy Lamour (wish we'd seen more of that).DiCaprio gives an Oscar-worthy performance, and Arnie Hammer is excellent as Tolson, teetering on the edge of camp (though he gets short-changed in the old-boy make-up). Dame Judi is good as always, and Naomi Watts makes Edgar's tirelessly loyal secretary an intriguing character. The film has a palette so muted (too muted?) it often seems like black-and-white. Eastwood's direction, like the script, is solid and makes a real effort to unravel the enigma that Hoover was, but the movie comes very close to being leaden in parts. Hoover served eight presidents: does this make him too sacred a cow to be put thoroughly through the revisionist meat-grinder?</fullReview>

DiCaprio can only carry J.Edgar so far though and Eastwood's attempt at defining the man as a humorless Darth Vader most of the way adds nothing revelatory to the anti myth of the past few decades that followed the legendary build-up of the 20s and 30's. 		<shortDescription>The Most Controversial American</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The portrait of one of the most controversial and feared men of the 20th century is brought to the screen with the cool, calculating precision of Clint Eastwood. J. Edgar Hoover was a man both admired and hated, a man who rose to be the most well known government figure to several generations of Americans; some loved him, some despised him. J. Edgar portrays the man in an honest and unflinching manner, chronicling the life of the creator of the FBI.Leonardo DiCaprio plays Hoover, giving his best performance in years. From the idealistic up-and-coming moralist to the embittered and paranoid old man, DiCaprio completely absorbs himself into the character. He brings Hoover's repressed homosexuality to the forefront of his portrayal. Every movement, every expression is done cautiously, as if Hoover was completely preoccupied with guarding his secret. The man who had dirt on everyone had a secret more taboo than anything else.Hoover's love interest is played by Armie Hammer, who is wonderful. He essentially provides the concise to Hoover's ambition, and as a result he is slowly silenced. Barbara Hershey is amazingly creepy as Hoover's overbearing and domineering mother who controls his life until the day she dies. "I'd rather have a dead son than a daffodil for a son" she tells Hoover.Eastwood directs the film in a cool, slow-paced manner, focusing on each scene to make every one of them important. The slowness may infuriate some, but this reviewer found it refreshing after a summer of Transformers. If there is any fault with the movie, it lies with Dustin Lance Black's script. He doesn't seem to be able to heighten the drama, and fails to pack a punch in some moments. But Black's use of the Lindberg kidnapping case as the dividing line between Hoover the idealist and Hoover the obsessed old man is well thought out.Overall, J. Edgar is a competent and intelligent film. Clint Eastwood does a remarkable job of recreating Hoover's world, and everyone in the main cast is Oscar worthy. The film succeeds by giving an unbiased and brutally honest look at one of the most hated men in American history.http://thatguythatlikesmovies.blogspot.com/</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Where Eastwood's strengths lack DiCaprio almost manages to make up for.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'J. EDGAR': Three and a Half Stars (Out of Five)Leonardo DiCaprio gives another mesmerizing performance in this biopic based on the famous FBI head honcho, for nearly 50 years, J. Edgar Hoover. He teams with director Clint Eastwood, who's directing turn is not nearly as impressive as DiCaprio's acting. The film as a whole is full of flaws and drags in narrative but DiCaprio's performance is still more than worth the price of admission and the subject matter is interesting despite the lack of quality storytelling. Eastwood has proved to be not quite as talented a director in recent years as many once thought he was but he still knows how to bring his trademark punch to all his work and, like I said, where his strengths lack DiCaprio almost manages to make up for. A film still worth seeing despite it's flaws (in my opinion).The film jumps back and forth from Hoover in his final days (DiCaprio in not so impressive makeup) to the time when he first took over the FBI and showed the world a new form of detective skills and law enforcement (including the tracking of fingerprints and knowing how to fully investigate a crime scene). The film focuses on his days working at the bureau as well as his private life, including his relationships with his overbearing mother (Judi Dench) and his close work associate Clyde Tolson (Armie Hammer, in even worse makeup as an old man). It centers on his struggles with his feelings for Clyde, as well as cross-dressing, and his battle to keep them hidden from the rest of the world. The film was written by Dustin Lance Black (who also wrote the critically acclaimed 'MILK', from two years ago).The movie is pretty slow moving and all over the place; like most biopics there's no real plot direction but this film seems even more aimless than most. Like I mentioned the makeup is pretty bad and other production values in the $35 million budgeted film seem surprisingly amateurish. Even besides DiCaprio the acting is all more than decent though and Eastwood gives the mood and feel his typically nice touch (although it's beginning to feel really familiar). Hoover is a very interesting character and they really couldn't have gotten a better actor to pull off the performance. This drives the film and definitely makes it worthwhile, despite it's flaws.Watch our review show 'MOVIE TALK' at: http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6V98a86sRto</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Recalcitrant Daffodil</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>J. EDGAR is a cinematic accomplishment of the first order! From the screenplay (Dustin Lance Black) as acted by a host of consummate actors and directed with extreme sensitivity by Clint Eastwood, the sum of the parts is an extraordinary achievement in presenting a portrait of one of the strangest men in history. The manner in which the life and deeds and personality are woven together presents as full an image of a man of contradictions, a man who planned to have his personal files destroyed after his death in order to maintain the iconic image he so desperately desired, is nothing short of a work of dedicated investigation on parts of everyone involved. And electing to tell this story through the ever-changing chameleon aspects of this bizarre man by shifting from youth to old age in a constant parenthetical manner was a stroke of genius.The rise of young John Edgar Hoover from a mother-favored child, through the emotional conflicts this mother worship produced, through the sightings of incidents suggestive of early 'Bolshevik' interference/threat in this country that Hoover focused on in his usurping power as the head of the newly formed Federal Bureau of Investigation, provides a background from which we can observe the morphing of this obsessed man. The act of staging press conferences around the Lindbergh case and the capture of the big criminals of the day were manipulated to give Hoover supreme power to investigate 'threatening people of interest' by wire taping, spying, etc. But all of this runs parallel to the personal story of Hoover's private sexual life. From his living with his mother until her death, to his incompetent attempt to force himself on the woman who would be his secretary throughout his career, to his bonding with Clyde Tolson who he chose to be his assistant and never was far from his side, to his final hours when Tolson though suffering from the residuum of a stroke was the only person Hoover could trust as Richard Nixon planned his destruction.Each of the incidents that highlighted Hoover's long career as founder and head of the FBI is presented well, but it is the de-mystification of Hoover the vulnerable man beneath the tyrannical faade that makes this film so extremely powerful. That is due to the script and the direction, of course, but it is also due in large part to the brilliant portrayal of this enigmatic beast of a pathetic man by Leonardo DiCaprio: he remains credible throughout and moves through the aging process and intermittent flashbacks to his younger days with complete ease and credibility. Likewise the performances of Judi Dench as his strange mother ('I'd rather my son be dead than be a daffodil' - her term for 'gay'), of Naomi Watts as his secretary who thwarts Hoover's initial awkward physical advances to become his lifelong supporter, and of Armie Hammer as Hoover's love interest Clyde Tolson acted with such a full range of emotion and subtlety that he is completely credible - these key roles are all of Oscar caliber as supporting roles. Eastwood's careful casting of actors for the roles of Charles Lindbergh (Josh Lucas), Eisenhower (Gunner Wright), Franklin Roosevelt (David A. Cooper), Harlan Fiske Stone (Ken Howard), Robert Kennedy (Jeffrey Donovan), Colonel Schwarzkopf (Dermot Mulroney), Bruno Hauptmann (Damon Herriman), Richard Nixon (Christopher Shyer), and Haldeman (Larkin Campbell) gives the audience the opportunity to catch glimpses of the people influenced by Hoover without stooping to caricatures. But in the end it is the slow dissection of the relationship between Hoover and Tolson that is the most powerful element of this film. The audience is left with little doubt that these men were, in truth, lovers without the expected obvious stereotypical activities and conversations. It is an act of greatness on the parts of all involved, and for this reason probably more than any other aspect of this huge film, this biopic deserves to be in the lineup for Best Picture of the Year. Grady Harp</fullReview>

		<fullReview>An attempt on a biography is indeed a perilous task. To take on a bio- pic indefinitely requires heightened attributes of courage and confidence. However this name would only be one that is familiar to the audience provided one is from a culture of similar geographical location. Eastwood is considering the fact that not everyone is as fluent of J.EDGAR as need be. This alone is one of the many reasons why I am rating this movie in one of the higher ranks. A biography made for everyone who is keen to learn the story. John Edgar Hoover was a law graduate who began to serve his nation at the department of justice and thereon worked his way through to the higher ranks and was instilled as the 6th director of the Bureau of Investigations (BOI) of the United States of America. Mr. Hoover was tough man who saw a threat to the nation in almost everyone in almost everything thing they did; a man who would stop at nothing and would go to the furthest extent to protect the nation and its citizens. Mr. Hoover was one with a vision of centralizing a finger print file, a valued source of data that would aid in fighting crime and controlling discipline. Although his ideas were harshly put aside on many occasions his sheer perseverance found him instituting the finger print system and the forensic laboratories. 11 years down the road as director of the BOI Mr. Hoover founded the Federal Bureau of Investigation thus being the first director of the worldly popular FBI.Leonardo DiCaprio who plays J.Edgar is a tyrant. DiCaprio has matured long since his early years and is undoubtedly living the role. DiCaprio seem to be taking every movie he does over the ropes. It's disheartening that he didn't get a nod at the Oscars for this exploding performance. His versatile acting of both the senior version of J.Edgar and the young adamant Edgar is spotless. The ferocity of the protagonist is uncanny. DiCaprio plays Edgar in a tone where it's hard to place his characters mood which adds a subtle anxiety to the viewer. The rise and the fall of the great director of the FBI was lived by DiCaprio and was elegantly aided by Armie Hammer who plays his number 2 guy and soul mate; Clyde Tolson. Wide historical speculation states that Mr. Hoover and Mr.Tolson at the time may have had a relationship that reaches beyond the norms of the bureau, how DiCaprio and Hammer bring that to life is convincing enough that I am not surprised that we do not know up-to date that it was in fact true. A viewer can never be certain if they were in fact gay, just as none could state for sure back in the day. A lion's share of the credit of the movie indeed deserves to focus on these gentlemen as they infuse its plot with a much needed essence of finesse.Judi Dench on the other hand could be considered as some talent that did go to waste. J.Edgar needs a strong mother who has the grace to stay afloat like a swan on the lake. Judi Dench indeed was nothing other than that. I somewhat preferred if she did have more screen-time however writer Justin Lance Black seem to have felt it was more appropriate to give the larger slice of the screen cake to Naomi Watts. Despite some unconvincing makeup on the veteran portrayal of Clyde Tolson every-other aspect of the picture is passable to a normal mature audience. I can't avoid the obvious. It's harmless to assume that the scales of the movie can always tip towards either side since it's a subjective arena. An audience with strong opinions on the life of Mr. Hoover and Mr.Tolson may disagree with a majority of this biography since it's obvious that Clint Eastwood is focusing purely on the stronger attributes of the gentleman. Such an audience may indeed disagree with even the DiCaprio performance as being unconvincing. With due respect to the Late Mr. John Edgar Hoover it's the only human approach as a director and as a writer to take the high road in making a biography.J.Edgar is a powerful motion picture with astute performances by some great actors and actresses and it unveils the compressed biography on one of the strongest and hard willed men to run the BOI /FBI. 		<shortDescription>Just a beautiful love story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Everybody knows that Clint Eastwood is Sergio Leone's artistic son. Everything in his shooting and editing style shouts this single fact at us. Mainly based on the Confucio's famous phrase ("Before you embark on a journey of revenge, you dig two graves"), Mr. Eastwood's work, in its entirety, relies on the concept that, whatever happens to you, your life is (or should be) an act of love. And "J. Edgar" is just that, a beautiful, aptly directed love story. They are wrong if they look for a police/FBI movie here. Wrong to seek for accuracy in the historic accounts. Very far from historians point of view about the subject of this movie. Largely away from a courtroom play. Far apart from everything but a nice, smooth, intelligent love story. This closet gay, this schizophrenic, obsessive, manipulative (and subtly manipulated) man that devoted his life as a whole to improve (according to him and almost nobody else) the USA homeland security, is trapped between his one and only professional interest and his deranged, unhappy, socially handicapped private life. He loves someone, somebody loves him as well, some people just tolerates him because they can get something in trade... And this situation perpetuates itself for weeks, months, years, decades and more decades.Eastwood directs this film in his usual, highly stylized manner; huge detail shots to remark some point, the fluid, ubiquitous steady cam job and his tender, meticulous cut-to-the-bone actors directing. He doesn't think Hoover was a completely sane man, and chooses to declare this (and his admiration for Hitchcock) through a scene that clearly resemblances "Psycho" (only with the Goldeberg variations instead of Herrmann's score and the "Eroica" number 9 record).This movie, great as it is, supports itself on Eastwood's visual goldsmithing, a powerful script and four extraordinary performances: Leonardo DiCaprio, Judi Dench, Naomi Watts and Armie Hammer (somewhat disfigured by a flawed makeup). Nice cameo by Lea Thompson (as Ginger Rogers' mother) and David Clennon (who starred in John Carpenter's "The Thing" as the ever-stoned pilot Palmer). Regarding to this, one of the most claustrophobic films ever made, there is a tiny tribute to Carpenter in the scene where Hoover talks sincerely with his mother.Looking for action in "J. Edgar" equals to look for boxing in "Million dollar baby": they are only movies about love, father-sister, mother-son, lover-lover, friend to friend and so on. This is what Mr. Eastwood masters the best.</fullReview>

		<username>Dharmendra Singh</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Visionary or Villain?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Eastwood is as great a director as he is an actor. His films strike that rare balance of being powerful and entertaining. 'Unforgiven', 'Mystic River' and 'Million Dollar Baby' are all, in my immodest opinion, five-star films. 'J. Edgar' is a three-star one which, on my chart, stacks up to better than mediocre, but not quite very good. Eastwood's film makes the same point Michael Mann's 'Public Enemies' did in 2009: John Edgar Hoover, who was head of the FBI from 1935 until his death in 1972, was a great administrator. He took pride in his ability to organise even a simple filing system. He never made arrest, never got his hands dirty. He was a pencil pusher, albeit an extraordinarily good one. But the man had unusual quirks and major flaws, which explains why Eastwood wanted to tell his story. Son to a senile father and a smothering mother, the young Edgar was tasked with establishing the family name. His first job was to root out suspected Commies. Because he loved his country to the point of jingoism, this was child's play. After Reds came John Dillinger, Machine-Gun Kelly and Baby-face Nelson: the bank robbers. Edgar said that he had to win the hearts and minds of the American people if they were to be successful in their mission. His belief in science as the weapon to fight crime was initially met with great suspicion, ridicule even. His idea of fingerprinting was initially dismissed as a 'speculative science'. The FBI's challenge wasn't limited to crime-fighting; they had an image problem. Kids wanted to hold up Tommy guns, not badges. Edgar did things to change that ? things that were scorned then, but lauded now. He insisted that the FBI needed to be independent of politics, that recruits should be chosen on merit, be college-educated and physically fit. He chastised anyone for wearing facial hair and anything but conservative suits. Old age normally tames even the most tyrannical figures. Edgar just got worse. He took vitamins as though he was an athlete, he worked obscene hours and he strengthened his suspicion of any perceived threats to his country and himself. Anyone was fair game. Presidents? Hell, he'd spy on their wives if he wanted to ? and he did. There's a comparison to be made with 'Citizen Kane'. Both films have the protagonist (better to say lead in these cases because they're not traditional heroes) rise through sheer ambition and then fall because of their spiralling megalomania and paranoia. Leo DiCaprio is predictably brilliant as Edgar. I am at a loss to think why he wasn't nominated for an Oscar. He gets into the skin of his eccentric character. Regrettably, however, the makeup team, usually the unsung heroes in movies, have made old Edgar look like a cheap waxwork. The standard of workmanship is egregious. Edgar was a mommy's boy. His mother (Judi Dench) had such a hold over him that he had no social life, couldn't dance, had no concept of how to court a lady, and perhaps couldn't be intimate with one. His purpose was to succeed professionally whatever the cost and that ambition was all for his mother. The scene where Edgar cross-dresses to remember her could have been farcical, but Eastwood directs a scene of sensitivity and brilliance. Then there's the issue of his sexuality. Was he or wasn't he? I thought I had made up my mind, but Eastwood makes you switch opinion with each scene. Armie Hammer plays Clyde Tolson, the one that people suspected of being Edgar's lover. Hammer, who has also fallen victim to the makeup artists (were they abstract?), is very good as Edgar's suffering number two man. Naomi Watts does well in a minor role as Miss Gandy, Edgar's servile PA. One thing I did like was how Eastwood shows the minutiae of a great man's ascension. A long time is spent on emphasising Edgar's punctiliousness because that was one of his signature qualities, something which separated him from others. DiCaprio gives a powerhouse performance in an average film, which is why he and Eastwood will miss out on Oscars this year.www.moseleyb13.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Strong Direction and Performances</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>J. Edgar (2011) *** 1/2 (out of 4) Another winner from director Clint Eastwood tells the story of the FBI and it's main force J. Edgar Hoover (Leonardo DiCaprio) starting in 1919 when the young Hoover first got the idea for such a place and we follow its rise to power as well as Hoover's self- centered importance to himself. Whenever a film based on a true person or events is released you usually get a side that comes out swinging saying how upset they are that certain aspects weren't covered or perhaps Hollywood put too much frosting on something or didn't shine a spotlight on a certain storyline. I'm sure many history buffs might find something else in Hoover's life more important but for the most part I thought the film was about as good as one could expect and this is due in large part to director Eastwood and star DiCaprio who certainly fit each other very well. I think that Eastwood make a wise decision in how he showed Hoover because it really does feel as if he just wanted to tell a story and never meant to show Hoover in a positive or negative light. If anything I'd say that the film is a lot more truthful and open than some people are giving it credit for because it certainly doesn't paint Hoover as some sort of God-like character and while it does show his greatness it never covers when it comes time to show some of the wrong that he did. The film cuts back and forth between the early days of the FBI and up to the Kennedy assassination, the stuff with Martin Luther King, Jr. and all the way up to Hoover's death. I think some of the most fascinating aspects deal with the kidnapping of Charles Lindburg's baby and how Hoover tried to use this as a way to get more money and more power. Another interesting aspect of the film is the relationship between Hoover and his right-hand man Clyde (Armie Hammer). The homosexuality aspect is certainly covered but I guess some will be expecting something a lot more graphic or in detail. Yes, there's a love story told between the two men but it's one of friendship more than anything and it's very nicely handled by Eastwood. Of course, one of the best things is getting to see DiCaprio add yet another great performance under his belt. It's really amazing to watch this guy act because once you get used to see him in the older make-up he really delivers a strong performance and one so great that you have no problem seeing Hoover and not an actor playing him. DiCaprio perfectly nails the accent and I thought the emotional range was terrific. The supporting cast is equally good and that include (Armie Hammer) as Clyde and (Naomi Watts) playing the personal secretary. If there are some flaws in the movie I think the biggest one is the make-up department. I thought the effects worked well on DiCaprio but some of the supporting characters looked so bad that it almost looked like a test rehearsal. Just check out the scene towards the end of the moving at a racing track when Clyde has something happen to him. I won't give away what but the make-up on him at this time looks so horrid that I was expecting it to slide off his face. Another minor problem is that I think too much is covered in a too short amount of time. The film clocks in at 137-minutes but it never feels too long and instead it almost comes off as being too short. With that said, J. EDGAR is a very entertaining movie and it's perfectly suited for someone like Eastwood. He perfectly captures the time and look of the past and DiCaprio delivers the performance to bring Hoover to life.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Clyde Tolson and John Edgar: A love story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The movie may as well have been called "Clyde Tolson and John Edgar: A Love Story." That love story is what the movie is about. When J. Edgar died in 1972--to allude to men holding hands, men kissing, men wearing dresses, men in long term asexual relationships--well that would be the stuff of Alfred Hitchcock. Who pursued whom in this relationship? And how much of the details of this relationship was verified by third-person eyewitness accounts? The pivotal moment in the film occurs when Hoover tells Tolson that there might be a future Mrs Hoover in the shape of Dorothy Lamour. Tolson goes ballistics, gets into fisticuffs with Hoover, bloodies his face, and finally kisses Hoover passionately. Hoover was either toying with Tolson, underestimated what their relationship was about, failed to recognize what that relationship was, or all of the above. And like so many people, Hoover was simply brain dead when it came to relationships. As astute a politician as he was, he could not discern subtleties of interpersonal relationships.It becomes clear with each of his interactions with the presidents, that the presidents would have preferred to get rid of him if they thought it would have been politically feasible. They each kept him waiting.By the time Hoover died in 1972, it was common knowledge that he had spied on many famous people: Eleanor Roosevelt, Frank Sinatra, Martin Luther King, and so on. This spying would reveal that President Kennedy and Chicago Mob Boss Sam Giancanni were sexually sharing the same women. At some point, Hoover crossed a line in domestic surveillance. What he found out about these people in that surveillance was their private sexual activities, and he used that knowledge to emotionally blackmail people. When John Kennedy is assassinated in 1963, Hoover is listening to a taped reel of a hotel tryst with some actress and some politician--the audience is intended to think John Kennedy and Marilyn Monroe.Hoover writes spurious letters critical of Martin Luther King's sexual affairs. He anticipates that because of these letters, King would decline the Nobel Prize. And tells Tolson as much as they watch television together. It was not only creepy, it suggested that Hoover was simply not-all-there as the saying goes. Psycho would be a more accurate term to describe Hoover.Clyde Tolson is not just J. Edgar's toady. He challenges Hoover in obtaining search warrants, he challenges Hoover's interpretation of events. The movie is a recollection by Hoover to various scribes; a reading Tolson challenges.The biggest flaw in the movie: the makeup. Actors wearing makeup to make them look older, is atrociously realized. They look like creatures from the walking dead. Whoever supervised this, approved this must have his head examined.I saw the movie within a context of Sept 11, 2001. "J Edgar" starts out with Attorney General Palmer's house being blown up and getting him and his family almost killed in June, 1919. Those were tumultuous years. The Reds, the Bolsheviks, wanted to bring down the country. The Lindberg baby kidnapping, the underworld run rampant during Prohibition--criminal behavior whose perpetrators had to be tracked down and brought to justice with the latest forensic tools. So the Patriot Act of George W Bush was a natural extension of Hoover's own policy for the common weal. And today with cyber-space being what it is, every keystroke, every telephone message, becomes traceable within minutes. By anyone with knowledge to hack into a computer, a telephone. Not just the police. What's the Fourth Amendment? What's a warrant? What's probable cause? What's the right to privacy? The Watergate Scandal would bring Nixon down; an important part of his argument: Nixon as President had carte-blanche to act as he did because it was not illegal since he was the President.But what's at stake in today's world? Some Terrorist planting a dirty nuclear bomb in the middle of Times Square or Washington, DC in Praise of Allah. When is the right to privacy annulled? What words have to be uttered to have those rights annulled? (Those are not quite rhetorical questions; we know what actions, what words are required for the intervention of the law.) "J. Edgar" struck a complex tone within that love story between Clyde Tolson and J. Edgar Hoover.</fullReview>

		<username>Luke Goldstein (goldwriting)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>The Crown Weighs Heavy on This One</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Every director certainly has a style and while some may try to shake things up every now and again, keep people on their toes, others stay the course and deliver time and time again what you have come to expect from them. That's not always a bad thing, especially when you have serious accolades and awards already under your belt, but it can also set up a certain type of expectation about the quality and depth of each story you bring to the screen, which sometimes can be a lot to overcome. The truth is there is no end to the sophomore curse. Your last fantastic picture is always quickly overshadowed by your current less than stellar outing. Clint Eastwood is the man under the spotlight right now and what he brings to the table is another tale of power, passion and persecution, all inside one continuously conflicted person.J. Edgar is one theory of the story behind the story, the man behind the machine that created the F.B.I. and reportedly had the skeletons of scores of American citizens, including the presidents he served under. The film follows his rise to power, his curious relationship with his number two man, and his own seemingly unquenchable need to be feared and revered, leaving a legacy that could never be tarnished.J. Edgar offers a scenario of what might have went on behind closed doors between Hoover and Tolsen, his number two man, and what motivated Hoover to push himself as hard as he did. Much of it is based on circumstance and conjecture though, so it'd be best to view this film as an imaginative or (at best) a mildly educated guess about the truth behind the most feared man in decades.Eastwood delivers yet again another deep, layered and complex narrative about a troubled protagonist, someone who you are never really sure whether you want to root for. The film is extremely slow paced and at times drags in its repetition, showing Hoover in one situation after another where his power is called into question. Jumping back and forth between his later life and his early years was a nice touch in the beginning, but by the end, it felt disjointed, like you were being dragged back into the past or thrust into the future just at the moment when things were getting good right where you were. I might have thought about just using the older version of Hoover as bookends to the story and play it out more along a traditional timeline, but who knows, that very well could have dragged as well.The performances are always the most important part of these types of biopics. You need to be able to lose sight of the actor, usually someone incredibly well known, and truly see the person he is trying to represent. Look at Frank Langella as Nixon in Frost/Nixon, Will Smith as Muhammad Ali in Ali, even our man here, Leonardo DiCaprio as Howard Hughes in The Aviator, these are invested performances that elevate the movie beyond just a mere educational stroll in the cinematic park. Yet, DiCaprio falters this time in capturing his past fervor, not for lack of trying, just due to a lack of foundation underneath the moments. Naomi Watts also struggles to really find footing as the dutiful secretary, Helen Gandy. The true breakout here is Armie Hammer as Tolson, who brings a magical assured quality to his early life and a beautiful gentleness in his senior years. Hammer truly burst onto the scene last year in his dual performance as the Winklevoss twins in The Social Network, but in J. Edgar he shows he can handle much more than just overconfidence. Tolson is really the moral compass of the film and the only avenue for the audience to navigate their way in, but even with such a virtuoso performance from Hammer, it wasn't enough to pull the whole film together in the end.Eastwood's decision to use younger actors in dramatically older roles also may not have worked to the film's advantage. I understand it allows a connection, both physical and emotional, between the two versions of the character on screen, but sometimes it can also feel jarring. While we have come light years ahead in the technology of makeup, truly transforming these early birds into aged senior citizens, the one thing that remains is the sound and tenor of their voice. There is something so unique and distinct about a voice that has been speaking for seventy or eighty years, something that is nearly impossible for these youthful actors to capture. Once again, Hammer seemed to outshine DiCaprio in this arena as well, but I still feel it might have been more powerful to have actual older actors in those roles.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Ruling by Frusrration</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In a world where appearances are that matter there was once a man who told people what they wanted to hear, ruled with an iron feast and cunning foresight. Whatever drove him, hunger for power, resentment over dreams he couldn't attain, self-delusion, or extreme frustration, we might never know, but he assembled a personal support system that allowed to him to succeed in a ways many can only dream of. He created an institution that is part of the American landscape; yet it's possible that no other leader might ever equal his compulsive desire for control.From the start, forces push J. Edgar, and aspirations to counter them develop. He focuses on development an organism that will allow him to set things straight, to fight evil, to be what he can't be otherwise, under the iron fist of a mother who can be both loving and suffocating because of her own personal beliefs. It can be argued she's both the source of his success and his personal nightmares. He's lucky enough to find an unwavering source of support, and although the film scratches the surface in elegant and subtle ways, one can probably guess that it was a very intimate relationship. Some of the best acting involves the quiet moments where the depth of such bond is explored. Actions are timid, careful, and once in a while, language dares to reveal a bit more.More successfully told than "Anonymous", the film goes back and forth in time, showing how Hoover gained power over the years, how his point of view attempts to control his audience, and the script is well put together that his spell works most of the time. The introductory scenes are a bit slow, but once we have become accustomed to Eastwood's pacing, we follow the seductive narrative that details how his brain worked, how his emotional side was a blend of torture and overcompensation, and there are hints of a repressed ability to care for others.Many wonderful scenes show a vulnerability many people probably never knew existed; still for the most part we see a man who hungers for acceptance, fame, control, and yet many are able to see through his insecurity but dare say nothing because he has become after all, a formidable force.Much is to be praised here, from Watts, De Caprio, and Hammer's work, to an unobtrusive musical score hinting at the not so obvious. The film is not perfect because there is so much that can't be explained, and once again the aesthetics threaten to overwhelm the substance, but it's hard to push Eastwood's reliable direction, and "Hoover" shows us he's possible, America's best movie director, a man who understands great movies originate from great scrips, great acting, and real confidence on the source, not on the dazzle of technique. This might not be "Million Dollar Baby" or "Mystic River", but it certainly stands out, and it's one of his best.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Underwhelming, sappy, and grossly inaccurate historical whitewash</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'J. Edgar (2011)' represents a serious disappointment and critical misfire by director Clint Eastwood and writer Dustin Lance Black.The film's crimes are many, but its most annoying offenses are: 1.) Devoting the bulk of its two plus hours to The Lindbergh kidnapping (a rather infinitesimal footnote in the Hoover story) while completely glossing over or ignoring Hoover's greater and darker legacy (his collusion with organized crime, the Nixon/Kissinger wiretaps, COINTELPRO, McCarthyism, etc., etc., etc.), and 2.) focusing on unfounded (but sensational) speculations about Hoover's supposed homosexuality and cross dressing.Numerous scenes between Hoover and Clyde Tolson simply defy credulity. By devoting large portions of the film to highly speculative private interactions (rather than what is historically known and documented), Black and Eastwood have fashioned together a non-credible account of one of America's most interesting (and loathsome) figures. They attempt to excuse themselves by suggesting that this is the story Hoover would want us to believe in that much of the narrative is framed by Hoover's dictation to his biographer, and in fact a late scene in the movie has Tolson literally accusing Hoover (and the film itself) of fabricating history. But these small efforts do not excuse the predictable confusion most audience members will experience nor the resulting mess of a movie the filmmakers have assembled.Compounded to the film's questionable accuracy is a rather groan-inducingly sappy tone, especially in the final act. Despite the character's contemptible standing, the audience is forced to endure a barrage of syrupy and sorrowful sequences upon Hoover's death, including disembodied words of reflection by Hoover and a sequence with Tolson crying while reading love poetry.On the plus side, Leonardo DiCaprio elevates the lackluster script and keeps the film generally watchable, or at least as watchable as can be expected beneath the weight of so many other shortcomings. Attempts are made to depict portions of Hoover's accomplishments, career, and legacy (including his contributions toward crime fighting techniques, willingness to blackmail sitting presidents, bloated paranoia of the communist threat - both real and perceived), etc.) Additionally, the minimal score is effectively sad and pretty, if not inappropriately manipulative. Unfortunately, these few positive points do not counter-balance the film's innumerable failings.At best, this film represents poor reporting, an incomplete story and a missed opportunity of the highest order. At worst, it's exploitation and outright bunk knowingly served as fact. A shamefully bad movie in either case.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Sadly, the new Warner Bros. epic is full of errors and misrepresentations. Here's the real, never-been-told inside scoop on the celebrated FBI Chief.Don't get me wrong. I'm a great fan of Clint Eastwood's...and I happen to like the guy. I worked closely with Clint on a screenplay some thirty years ago at Universal and again at Malpaso, his own company. Clint actually optioned "THE HOSTILES" twice in an effort to get it made with co-star John Wayne. Unfortunately, Duke did not sign on and Clint and I were both disappointed that the film never came to be. Over the years, I have had several cordial encounters with Clint so I have no ax to grind with him personally. He's a great filmmaker and a fine fellow. Unfortunately, his new movie about J. Edgar Hoover is so full of untruths that I can't sit idly by without making comment.I recently received a call from Leonardo DiCaprio's company, Appian Way, asking to see a copy of my movie about J. Edgar Hoover, which starred Broderick Crawford as Hoover and Dan Dailey as Clyde Tolson, with a splendid cast headed by Jose Ferrer, Celeste Holm and Rip Torn. It was a very well regarded film, particularly in England where it had a significant theatrical run plus two highly promoted telecasts on the BBC.In America, the film was a victim of the fears of film distributors that the FBI would retaliate against them for releasing a picture critical of the Bureau -- particularly at a time when the FBI anti-piracy seal was being affixed to every VCR put on the market. Still, the film was released by American International Pictures and received many terrific reviews.After sending a copy of my film to Mr. DiCaprio, I got a call to schedule a meeting with him -- but it was unexpectedly canceled and there was no follow-up. I could only assume that Leonardo had been advised by Warner's attorneys or by Clint himself to steer clear of me. (I might add I consider Leonardo a most gifted actor and I wish him nothing but success in this new role).Shortly after I was privileged to read Clint's screenplay on Hoover, I did receive a call from Mr. Rob Aurins, who identified himself as Clint's producing partner. His message expressed sincere interest in discussing what elements I might add to the new movie but after I returned the call, I never heard from him again. It's unfortunate since the new movie is full of inaccuracies and outright misrepresentations. (Read entire piece on my website)Sincerely,Larry Cohen</fullReview>
